# Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_318 messages · 18 participants · 2007-07 → 2007-08_

**Topics:** [`Language features and syntax`](../topics.md#syntax)

---

### Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

- **From:** Lizette Koehler <starsoul@mindspring.com>
- **Date:** 2007-07-23T13:38:04-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`

```
I have just joined and am trying to determine what caused 1 paragraph
out of many to do a fall though as opposed to going back up and
executing the next instruction.  I suspect that it has something to do
with a missint END-IF/

We are running Enterprise COBOL 3.1 on z/OS V1.7.

So I was wondering if there were any tools that can match up nested
code.  Such  PERFORM with EXIT and IF  END-IF.

Lizette
```

#### ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2007-07-23T16:07:35-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<46A4D247.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`

```
>>> On 7/23/2007 at 2:38 PM, in message
<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>, Lizette
Koehler<starsoul@mindspring.com> wrote:
> I have just joined and am trying to determine what caused 1 paragraph
> out of many to do a fall though as opposed to going back up and
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> code.  Such  PERFORM with EXIT and IF  END-IF.

One thing you can look at is the 'level number' (the SL column) in the
compile listing.

Take, for example, this very contrived example:

000008         000008     IF A > 4        
000009      1  000009         CONTINUE    
000010         000010     ELSE            
000011      1  000011     IF A > 3        
000012      2  000012         CONTINUE    
000013      1  000013     ELSE            
000014      2  000014     IF A > 2        
000015      3  000015         CONTINUE    
000016      2  000016     ELSE            
000017      3  000017     IF A > 1        
000018      4  000018         CONTINUE    
000019      3  000019     ELSE            
000020      4  000020         PERFORM DOIT
000021      3  000021     END-IF          
000022      2  000022     END-IF          
000023      1  000023     END-IF          
000024         000024     END-IF          

As you can see, the first IF (the one with no level number) is terminated by
the final END-IF.  If there is a level number next to what appears to be the
'final' END-IF then you can be pretty certain and END-IF is missing
somewhere.

Though I'm not sure off the top of my head how a missing END-IF could cause
the symptoms you are experiencing.  Since a paragraph *must* end with a full
stop (period) then, at the very least, any previous IF statement would be
terminated at that point.  Is it possible that you are falling through to
the beginning of the perform thru group?  For example:

PROCEDURE DIVISION.
START-HERE.
    PERFORM BEGIN-PARA THRU EXIT-PARA.

BEGIN-PARA.
    DISPLAY 'BEGINNING'.

MIDDLE-PARA.
    DISPLAY 'MIDDLE'.

EXIT-PARA.
    EXIT.

DROPPED-THRU.
    DISPLAY 'UNEXPECTED?'

This is too obvious an error to make (missing a STOP RUN at the end of the
START-HERE paragraph), but something like it is often the cause of this kind
of problem.  Of course it has the obvious symptom (in this case) of the
output being:
BEGINNING
MIDDLE
BEGINNING
MIDDLE
UNEXPECTED?
But if your code isn't doing displays or something else that makes it easy
to see, you might miss it.

Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T12:36:59-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Jul 24, 8:38 am, Lizette Koehler <stars...@mindspring.com> wrote:

> I have just joined and am trying to determine what caused 1 paragraph
> out of many to do a fall though as opposed to going back up and
> executing the next instruction.  I suspect that it has something to do
> with a missint END-IF/

It is very unlikely to be 'missing END-IF'.

If you have code that is

    PERFORM A THRU A-exit
    ..

   A.
     ...
        GO TO A-exit
     ...
   A-exit.

then there are several programming errors available to you to cause a
drop-thru.

1) forgetting the THRU:-  "PERFORM A"
   The GO TO A-Exit will take you beyond the scope of the perform.

2) Using the wrong exit:- "PERFORM A THRU B-exit"
   The code will drop thru until it finds B-exit somewhere.

3) GO TO the wrong exit:- "GO TO X-exit"
   In A a GO TO outside the range will cause mysterious action.

4) Duplicate exit paragraph:- Two A-exit paragraphs.
   The GO TO goes to the wrong one.

The main problem is that the compiler cannot tell you that these
errors exist. The checking must be done manually and exhaustively or
by a program processing the code and checking against a set of
restrictive standards.

Being lazy and not wanting to do this type of checking I avoid all
need for it by using code that never requires it. ie No SECTIONs, no
GOTO, no THRU, no EXIT PERFORM/PARAGRAPH/SECTION, no NEXT SENTENCE.
```

```
On Jul 28, 12:03 pm, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:

> Man, don't start a religious style war again.  SECTIONS are never necessary,
> so far as I know, but can be used perfectly effectively.
>
> Evrybody has a verb or two that they dislike.  Who cares????  Does it work?
> Is it easy to maintain?

SECTIONs do have their uses, for example segmentation. As I pointed
out above I dislike things that can easily lead to errors and that the
compiler won't tell you about. In fact I would use that as a
definition of what is _not_ 'easy to maintain' and _not_ 'perfectly
effective'.

For example (as above) the simple absence of one SECTION word on a
label can cause a drop thru error that can be difficult to locate and
which the compiler gives no warning of.

The only purpose for using PERFORMed SECTIONs instead of having all
the labels as paragraphs is to allow the use of exit paragraphs and
the associated GO TOs. Thus this style suffers the errors in my other
messages of this topic, ones that the compiler cannot find for you.

Perhaps you find that these errors are simply a part of 'normal
programming' and find them easy, after all what would you do for the
rest of the week if they did not occur.

> Does it follow the installation standards?
> Discussion over!

Yes, that is often used to protect poor programming styles.
```

While testing may well find many programming errors it is unlikely to
find _all_ of them. For example if there is a GOTO X-exit in the scope
of a PERFORM A THRU A-exit (due to copy-paste from another) then it
will only be 'found' if that particular branch is actually tested.  As
many of these may be rare errors or exceptions it may not be found
even in live running for months.

Of course automated tools can be used to find these if they have been
done in a standard way. Was it Judson who had this.

of those exact programming errors. It was a specific message to them
to check for those errors. I also stated how _I_ avoided them
_without_ claiming that is was "the only way".

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2007-07-27T23:42:30-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
[snip]
> The only purpose for using PERFORMed SECTIONs instead of having all
> the labels as paragraphs is to allow the use of exit paragraphs and
> the associated GO TOs.

Well, no, that is not the 'only' purpose! Another is
encapsulating mini-programs as parts of a larger program.
This is similar to nested programs; but without the call
overhead or the structuring requirements for contained
and common programs. The first paragraph terminates
with EXIT SECTION rather than EXIT PROGRAM
and all paragraphs, except the first, within each section
are performed only by other paragraphs within the same
section; though some statements may perform other
sections. It is EXIT SECTION that allow this to be
accomplished without exit paragraphs or GO TO
statements. <g>

I actually did this once, with a slight variation, to isolate
the code for a recursive quicksort.
-----
       quick-sort section.
            ...
           call "qsort" using i j
           exit section
           .
           entry "qsort" using l r
            ...
           exit program
           .
        [performed paragraphs]
        [next section]
-----
The implementation does not allow recursive nested
programs.

[Incidentally, this is the same program, I had mentioned
some time ago, as having one section where only GO TO
statements were used for flow control within that section.]
```

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

On Jul 28, 3:42 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> statements. <g>

EXIT SECTION/PARAGRAPH are just GO TOs to an unnamed paragraph.

You could accomplish exactly the same thing without the SECTION word

was a group of related paragraphs.

That is to say that it does not 'encapsulate' in any way, nor provide
any additional safety or other features beyond simply using paragraph
labels (and comments).  It does however allow the use of a GO TO
within the scope of the PERFORM though you have replaced this with a

out of a PERFORM VARYING into its own paragraph or vv.  Or splitting
down a nested IF into small paragraphs. Any statement that does not
allow this or needs to be changed when moving code I do not use.

For example in your first paragraph if you had a nested IF that had an
EXIT SECTION it would be an error to move that code into a paragraph
(or another SECTION) and PERFORM it.

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>

- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com...
> On Jul 28, 3:42 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> > "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> EXIT SECTION/PARAGRAPH are just GO TOs to an unnamed paragraph.

There are technical reasons why the various EXITs are
not "just GO TOs". I know because it was one of my
contributions to finalizing the 2002 standard. The
introduction of the "unnamed paragraph" as the target
for EXIT SECTION was necessary to distinguish
between the transfer mechanism at the end of the last
paragraph of the section and the one at the end of the
section, itself. These are the transfer mechanisms used
by PERFORM, for example. That the EXITs are seen
as "just GO TOs" seems to show how well the
technicalities were addressed.

> You could accomplish exactly the same thing without the SECTION word
> and without the EXIT SECTION. A comment line could indicate that this
> was a group of related paragraphs.

Others, maybe; but not me! I would accomplish it in
exactly the same way because I am comfortable with
sections and EXIT SECTION.

> That is to say that it does not 'encapsulate' in any way, nor provide
> any additional safety or other features beyond simply using paragraph
> labels (and comments).  It does however allow the use of a GO TO
> within the scope of the PERFORM though you have replaced this with a
> _slightly_ better EXIT SECTION.

Well, as previously discussed, one reason I use sections
is to allow the use of GO TO; but only within the section.
Thus the section exists as if it were a capsule; such that
GO TO statements occur only within that which is
encapsulated.

> I like to move code from one place to another. For example taking it
> out of a PERFORM VARYING into its own paragraph or vv.  Or splitting
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> (or another SECTION) and PERFORM it.

If I used EXIT SECTION in an IF, EVALUATE, or other
conditional statement, it would not likely be nested and,
therefore, would not need to moved.

    IF  condition
        EXIT SECTION
    END-IF

> >
> > I actually did this once, with a slight variation, to isolate
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> And in what way does this make it 'more maintainable' ?

When sections are used as mini-programs, they have
a well-defined interface, thus all changes to the
mini-program are isolated from the rest of the code.
And, such mini-programs may be easier to re-use.

When sections are used in the same manner as
paragraphs, they do not necessarily have what may
qualify as an interface and the lack of isolation allows
for the same ad hoc coupling that may exit with
paragraphs only.
```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

No! In particular, paragraph-names need only be unique
within a section. Others may use paragraph-names that
are unique within the program (for example, a prefix on
every procedure-name) and thus obtain some degreee
of equivalence. Since I am not concerned, generally, with
paragraph-names outside the section, any attempt to
modify "my" programs to remove the section keyword
could result in paragraph-name conflicts.

I don't know that sections "would make the compiler do
better checking"; but I believe it helps me to do better
writing.

> > > That is to say that it does not 'encapsulate' in any way, nor provide
> > > any additional safety or other features beyond simply using paragraph
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> impose with no help from the language or compiler.

Except that the compiler will resolve conflicting
paragraph-names to the same section containing the
GO TO statement.

> > > I like to move code from one place to another. For example taking it
> > > out of a PERFORM VARYING into its own paragraph or vv.  Or splitting
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> manual intervention for what I see as trivial restructuring.

Perhaps there is some confusion concening EXIT SECTION
versus EXIT PARAGRAPH. As it applies to COBOL 2002,
EXIT SECTION may occur anywhere within the section to
the same effect. It is moving EXIT PARAGRAPH that may
create some problems; but EXIT PARAGRAPH is unrelated
to the use of sections.

> > > > I actually did this once, with a slight variation, to isolate
> > > > the code for a recursive quicksort.
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> they use.

Paragraph-names are isolated. Isolation of local or
private data requires a naming convention.

> > When sections are used in the same manner as
> > paragraphs, they do not necessarily have what may
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> merely the way you order the code.

Well, there is a difference in regard to paragraph-names.
And, ...

    move data to section-interface-input
    perform section-name
    move section-interface-output to data

is as real an interface as the equivalent call to a separate
program. It is in the defining of the interface as actual
data description entries that makes the interface real
and, while I suppose some discipline may be required to
prevent ad hoc coupling, I see it as less of an imposition
when using sections. I think this is because procedural
programming is about procedures and using sections as
major procedures implemented by paragraphs as minor
procedures stands out better than paragraph groups.

```
On Jul 30, 2:25 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> within a section.

I will grant you that paragraph names only need be unique within a
section and that it is not necessary to qualify the name. However,
this need not be a 'good thing', the compiler may not warn about
'missing' or misspelled labels. For example if two sections were
supposed to have the same paragraph name but one became corrupted or
was misspelled then the code would silently use the wrong one. There
is no actual 'encapsulation'.

I would also say that this was a really useful feature in the 60s when
huge monolithic programs had to be built by several programmers
because CALL was not available (it was added to ANS'74). Now there are
actual mechanisms that do real encapsulation.

> Others may use paragraph-names that
> are unique within the program (for example, a prefix on
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> could result in paragraph-name conflicts.

At least the compiler would tell you where those were.

> I don't know that sections "would make the compiler do
> better checking"; but I believe it helps me to do better
> writing.

Yes, encapsulated code and defined interfaces is better writing.
Grouping related code is better writing. But you are not using any
compiler features to enforce this as would happen with sub-programs or
classes, it is better writing by manually imposed discipline.

> Except that the compiler will resolve conflicting
> paragraph-names to the same section containing the
> GO TO statement.

Some years ago I had sent a proposal to J4 that suggested the addition
of label qualifiers. For example adding a LOCAL qualifier to a
paragraph label would ensure that it could only ever be referenced
from within the enclosing section *. While, as you say, the compiler
will resolve first of all to the same section, if by error or
misspelling it does not exist, the compiler will then resolve to
wherever it can can find the name and this may not cause an warning or
error.

In any case I no longer regard 'GO TO' as better programming, not
since ANS'85.

> Perhaps there is some confusion concening EXIT SECTION
> versus EXIT PARAGRAPH. As it applies to COBOL 2002,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> to the use of sections.

You are probably correct. I decided years ago that these were
unnecessary in the style of coding that I use and were merely another
way of doing a GOTO while pretending not to. In any case it is
unlikely that I will ever use a 2002 compiler.

> > But is only a manually imposed so-called 'mini-program'. There is
> > nothing in the language that assists them. as for example is done with
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> private data requires a naming convention.

No. Paragraph names are _not_ actually isolated. There is nothing
preventing code in one section to perform a paragraph in another. It
may be that the compiler will attempt to resolve to a local name
first.

> > Sticking the word SECTION on it makes zero difference, it is only your
> > self-imposed discipline that creates this alleged 'isolation' and lack
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>     move section-interface-output to data

> is as real an interface as the equivalent call to a separate
> program.

No more so than PERFORM paragraph-name where that was the same first
paragraph to a related group of paragraphs. But then you wouldn't be
able to GOTO xx-exit or exit section, which is the difference that I
said in the first place.

> It is in the defining of the interface as actual
> data description entries that makes the interface real

Exactly, it has nothing to do with the use of the SECTION word.

> and, while I suppose some discipline may be required to
> prevent ad hoc coupling, I see it as less of an imposition
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> procedures stands out better than paragraph groups.

I think that mixing performs of sections with perform of paragraphs is
poor programming and I have no problem grouping paragraphs together
into related groups without using the SECTION word.

I can think of many features that would assist in ensuring this but
the word SECTION does none of them.


* The qualifiers that I proposed (that I can recall) were FROM,
PERFORMED, ABOVE, LOCAL.

LOCAL meant only accessible from within the section. FROM meant it
could be subject of a GO TO. ABOVE meant it could be dropped into from
the previous paragraph. PERFORMED meant it could be performed. If any
qualifier(s) were used it restricted the program to those specified.
Example:

      ABC SECTION PERFORMED.
      ABC-1 ABOVE LOCAL.
           ...
           GO TO ABC-Exit.
      ABC-2 PERFORMED LOCAL.
           ...
      ABC-Exit FROM LOCAL.
         EXIT.

ABC could only be performed. Drop thru into it would cause run time
error.
Drop thru into ABC-2 or ABC-exit also run time error.
GO TO ABC-exit from anywhere outside the section is compiler error.
ABC-1 can only be dropped into (from the perform of ABC).

However, J4 quite rightly rejected this as there were better ways of
encapsulating.
```

```
On Sun, 29 Jul 2007 10:25:28 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
wrote:

>Perhaps there is some confusion concening EXIT SECTION
>versus EXIT PARAGRAPH. As it applies to COBOL 2002,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>to the use of sections.

The primary thing that EXIT PARAGRAPH does in the discussion of
SECTIONs is to get rid of the argument that sometimes maintenance is
easier with a GO TO MY-EXIT command. 

So now we don't need any type of PERFORM THRU commands.  All of the
programming of SECTIONS that I have seen since around 1980 have been
for implicit PERFORM THRU commands, not counting the old SORT
requirement.

When we get rid of all implied or explicit PERFORM THRU, never
dropping from paragraph to paragraph, then paragraph position doesn't
matter except for the very first paragraph.   

With this philosophy, my compiler can mean some ugly switches when a
maintenance programmer wants to get out of a paragraph with minimal
programming changes - but yours doesn't have this problem.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2007-07-30T18:23:18-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13aspac4a3nak82@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <1185694700.155282.17800@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ap8un6dih5ibf@corp.supernews.com> <3t2sa3lfvujertcmoi4uavo50dnjs3gk30@4ax.com> <13asdrinb8g75c6@corp.supernews.com> <s6fsa35iirm4ehc1gr23r4e9i2jj9m32ib@4ax.com>`

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:s6fsa35iirm4ehc1gr23r4e9i2jj9m32ib@4ax.com...
> On Mon, 30 Jul 2007 15:07:35 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> reasons such as memory segmentation, and sorts).

It seems that our experiences are different, which may
explain why you have questions.

> This thread proposed that it should also be used to achieve some of
> the advantages of subprograms or OO, by counting on programmers being
> disciplined.    In that case, why not use subprograms or OO?

The value of program, function, and method definitions
lies in the ability to use the resulting entities more than
once. The question then is how is one to determine what
common functionality to include in those separate entities.
One may use analysis techniques for some cases,
particularly for those that are recognizable; and one may
use design techniques for those that are not
well-understood. Using design as the phase for creating
and describing these "mini-programs" may result in more
reusable code, once the common functionality emerges.

I also mentioned "ad hoc coupling". This goes hand-in-hand
with ad hoc design. How is one to determine what interfaces
and functionality may be present if everything is the result of
"because I felt like it". This includes placing code into separate
entities "because it seems a good thing to do".
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-07-29T23:56:46+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com>`

```


"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message 
news:13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com...
>
> "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com...
>> On Jul 28, 3:42 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

<snipped rehash of old debate ...:-)>
>
> When sections are used as mini-programs, they have
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I was impressed with these two paragraphs.

The first one is something I do (did?) all the time. It is particularly 
obvious when you use OO, although I hadn't thought about it in the terms 
described.

I think the second paragraph may have identified why there is such divided 
opinion over the use of SECTION. Many people see it in the same terms they 
see a paragraph name. Those of us who have used it successfully for many 
years (without being shot, or even frowned at, by people subsequently 
required to maintain our code), do not use it in that fashion.

I have never used PERFORM XXX-SECTION THRU xx-EXIT and I never would. Using 
SECTION makes the THRU redundant.

As for cutting and pasting bits of source code around a program, no I don't 
do that either. If you need it in more than one place, put it in a SECTION 
and PERFORM it (Or consider making it a called subprogram).

I think this second paragraph is an insightful comment on use of SECTIONS. 
(Given that this topic has been thrashed to death here over the years, this 
is the first time I have seen anything posted that gave a different insight 
or actually added something of value.)

For myself, I was forced to move from PERFORM THRU (paragraph names) to 
PERFORM [Section] when the standards in the place I was working at were 
changed by a new chief programmer who liked SECTIONS. (This was around 
1968). Very explicit rules were laid down about their use (no mixing 
PERFORMs of paragraphs and sections in the same program, no use of THRU, 
Sections being used to encapsulate specific functions, and so on. Most of us 
were not happy with the change; people usually resist what they're not used 
to, but within a few months most of us had got the "hang of it" and had no 
problem with it. Since then I have always written SECTIONed code in COBOL 
and it has never been a problem (for me, or for others who had to 
subsequently maintain the code.)

Yes, it CAN be problematic (so can many COBOL constructs when used loosely) 
but it can also be useful.

Why it is necessary to have a jihad over it or call for the shooting of 
people who do it, is beyond me...:-) I have mantained code written by others 
that used every kind of variation  and I don't recall ever complaining about 
it. That's what they paid me to do. I'm a professional programmer; give me 
code that's broken and I'll fix it.(There was a time when I wasn't the only 
one.... :-))

So often here I see people bleating about "ease of maintenance" when what 
they really mean is "Write it the way I do, then it 's easy to maintain"... 
They don't usually add the "...for me" that that particular sentence 
actually requires.

Oh, did I mention I also maintained code that had ALTERs in it?  Never had a 
problem with that particular construct, either, and on early platforms where 
we cared about bytes and performance, ALTER was an extremely efficient way 
of implementing things like first time switches and managing overlays. I was 
sorry to see it go, but the wimps won the day... :-)

I have become persuaded against GO TO over the years, but it really doesn't 
bother me if others use it. After maintaining and managing spaghetti code 
for years before Mr. Djikstra wrote his paper, I was pretty blase about it.

These days components rather than source code are more the focus of my 
effort, so I really don't care about source code wars, or people 
pontificating on what is "right" or "wrong" regarding technique, in a 
near-obsolete programming language.

PL got it right when he says that  what works is what matters.

Anyway, thanks to Rick, whose post shed some light on WHY this is a 
sensitive topic.

Pete.

"I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

I recall very little about the source for these ideas, except
a distinct memory concerning using the same prefix for
both the section-name and data-items in working-storage,
and that program documentation for each section was limited
to describing the interface and functionality to be achieved.
Other authors I have read had horrendous requirements for
documentation and used technical (or academic), rather than
practical, explanations for their recommendations for using
perform thru and perform paragraphs only. I felt they were
talking at me instead of to me.

With practice I became quite comfortable with (and with the
idea of) using sections in every way they can be used.

- **Date:** 2007-07-30T20:49:15-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185853755.358016.273420@g12g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net> <3i3sa3hcmfal9nepkknnke9cq2eu4981c5@4ax.com> <5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com>`

```
On Jul 31, 2:38 pm, LX-i <lxi0...@netscape.net> wrote:

> OK - here's a question for you old guy... er, seasoned professionals...
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> ALTER in a good way?

At the time of Cobol being designed in the late 50s it was quite
common for assembler programmers to use self-modifying code as a way
of avoiding the overhead of testing flags. In fact with the first
machines I used the PERFORM actually did work by modifying a branch at
the end of a PERFORM scope to jump back to after the PERFORM and then
branched to the start of the scope. The 'EXIT' noise word in its own
paragraph was required in very early compilers because that reserved
space for the branch that the PERFORM set.

ALTER was implementing the standard mechanism in common use.

If you want to see something complicated try to get the book 'Early
British Computers' where it describes programming for mercury delay
tubes and gives an example.

Because the cycle time of the memory was much slower than the
instruction execution speed it was necessary to do several
instructions in one memory cycle.  So every instruction specified the
next instruction address, it would be far to slow to have to wait for
the next address to come round again. 10 would be, say, an add of
address 15 to the 'mill' and next instruction at 30. The instruction
would be able to complete this and pick up the value, complete the add
and pick up 30. 30 would store the accumulator in 38 and next
instruction 47.

The program had to be laid out in a map and timings done to get it to
work at best speed. Core store allowing sequential instructions and
self-modifying code (eg ALTER) was an enormous improvement in
programming.
```

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** Binyamin Dissen <postingid@dissensoftware.com>
- **Date:** 2007-08-02T16:30:50+03:00

An example that struck me as sensible at the time was to handle EOF in an
out-of-line file read: it's a long time ago so I don't recall the exact
details but it was something like this:

READ.

READ FILE AT END MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO FILE-KEY
                                   ALTER READ TO PROCEED TO READ-X
                                    GO TO READ-X.
(A bunch of other code)
READ-X.
EXIT.

As I say I can't recall the details; this construct did solve whatever the
problem statement was and did not cause too much strain on the maintainer
since the target of the ALTER was right there.  I do remember being a bit
nervous about the compiler's ability to handle the paragraph-name READ both
as a PERFORM target and an ALTER target, but there were never any problems.

PL

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5h97ekF3hv8npU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net> <3i3sa3hcmfal9nepkknnke9cq2eu4981c5@4ax.com> <5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com>`

```


"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Now, see, that's a really good question. So much more interesting than
simply arguing about the relative merits of ALTER.:-)

I'll attempt to give you an answer that excludes the things already covered,
like first time thru switches, although these are worth looking at for a
moment, in passing...

If you put an IF statement at the start of  a piece of code (maybe even a
SECTION... :-)) that says:

IF FIRST-TIME-THRU
     SET NOT-FIRST-TIME-THRU TO TRUE
     PERFORM TAKE-THE-LID-OFF
END-IF
PERFORM POUR-A-MEASURE
...

And this section gets executed  (by PERFORM or GO TO) 10 million times, you
are doing a compare that can never be true 9,999,999 times. Nowadays, that
isn't such a big deal, but there was a time when it was...

If, instead, you were to formulate it as:

FIRST-TIME.
      GO TO REMOVE-LID.
REMOVE-LID.
       ALTER FIRST-TIME TO PROCEED TO OTHER-TIMES
       PERFORM TAKE-THE-LID-OFF.
OTHER-TIMES.
       PERFORM POUR-A-MEASURE
...

You have removed 9,999,999 instructions from the execution. Now it just does
a branch instead of a compare. On most platforms this is the quickest
instruction in the set. (Almost invariably, a single machine instruction).
Compares on some machines can be horrific, generating up to a dozen machine
instructions (especially if data conversion is required before the compare
can take place.)

Can you honestly say that the second example above is much more complex than
the first? Or so "horrendous" it couldn't be maintained by a trained COBOL
Programmer? :-)

It doesn't even take much more writing, yet it saves 9,999,999 compare
instructions, and is maybe 12 times more efficient than your IF switch....

Some people would call that "good" :-)

YOUR QUESTION:

> Now my curiosity is more theoretical.  How does one think to design ALTER
> in a good way?

It isn't really about designing ALTER, it is about approaching things in a 
different way.

I'll rephrtase your question thus:

"So, what kind of mindset do you need to deal with COBOL that
modifies itself as it runs?"

MY ANSWER:

Something close to the old Assembler mindset where instructions in memory
were frequently modified (not just the Branch instructions) to save time and
space.

You need a non-linear mindset that is imaginative and intelligent. What
DeBono calls "Lateral" thinking,  but not only must you think "outside the
box" you must do it "on the hop".   Some people really enjoy this way of
working and are very good at it, most do not and are not... It is definitely
not for the faint-hearted...(That's what I meant when I said "the wimps won
the day") :-)

Now let's look at a few cases where the ALTER solution is probably the most
elegant one available...

Consider a dynamic process where a series of  "stages" are executed in a
variable sequence, but only as the execution proceeds, can the subsequent
stage requirements be decided. Normally, we would write a program that
controlled the flow, tracked each item through each stage,  and could cater
for every possible "path". That would be linear.

But what about when the "path" is actually a network and the possibilities
are approaching infinity? We have little choice but to cause the stages to
be switched as we proceed through them. "Fast switching"...rings a bell...
sure there used to be something in COBOL that could do that...Oops! Not any
more.

What about a state transition processor?  You have a bunch of, say, 700 odd
keywords for COBOL and you need to parse a COBOL source program. You COULD
lookup every word the parser encountered against the Reserved word list.
That would be linear. (It would also be pretty slow).

Suppose instead you had a series of transition diagrams









 be decided. . There are dependencies and there are exceptions. If we call
the "stages" (they could be machines on a shop floor, software functions or
objects,  people proceeding through immigration, whatever your imaginative,
intelligent mind can come up with...:-))  arrive that conflict with each
other. It is a dynamic process with different areas of the organisation all
independently submitting and correcting requirements. Let's say we have a
machine that cuts corrugated cardboard or carpet, or linoleum from huge
rolls, according to the sizes that people order. An order arrives, start
cutting... Well, you COULD do that, but it it would be pretty wasteful.







 Ghastly, or

> -- 
> ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> or a man who's offended by a God he doesn't believe in?" - Brad Stine
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ALTER design (Was: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through)

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-07-31T13:17:46-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1185913066.280005.293910@i13g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<5h97ekF3hv8npU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net> <3i3sa3hcmfal9nepkknnke9cq2eu4981c5@4ax.com> <5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com> <5h97ekF3hv8npU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Aug 1, 4:48 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> If, instead, you were to formulate it as:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> [snip]

> Can you honestly say that the second example above is much more complex than
> the first? Or so "horrendous" it couldn't be maintained by a trained COBOL
> Programmer? :-)

Yes, it is actually far more complex than the first.

It is more complex because it has added a temporal element into the
code. Although it appears to be static it changes with time during the
execution. While 'First-Time' has an apparently fixed and obvious
destination the actual destination varies from time to time.

It is fairly obvious when reading this few lines that it does change
at some point to a different destination. What is not apparent is that
it may be changed by other code elsewhere to somewhere completely
different, or back to its original setting.

The only way to determine how the program will actually run with any
particular set of data is the step through every instruction starting
from the top and recording which way the ALTERs set the paths as the
data is processed.

I once posted a proposed method of quantifying one type of complexity
in Cobol code. Basically it was a summation of the number of ways that
each label could be reached. For each label (paragraph or section) add
1 if it can be dropped through, and 1 if it subject to a GO TO and 1
if it is subject of a PERFORM and 1 if it is subject of a THRU.

For each label that is subject to an ALTER add 10,000.

As an aside one could see that the measurement could be reduced by,
say, adding an explicit GO TO ~exit at the end of the paragraph just
before this to eliminate the drop through.

eg    PERFORM A THRU A-exit   (or A section)

      A (or section)
         IF ... GO TO A-exit.

         GO TO A-exit.
      A-exit.
         EXIT.

This additional and 'unnecessary' GO TO eliminates the drop through
and thus reduces the 'complexity count' to 3 (if THRU) or 2 (if
section, though a first pargraph label would add 1 as a drop through).

This is reasonable because adding a paragraph before A-exit is now no
longer dropped through accidentally. The complexity of maintaining the
program is reduced by eliminating opportunities for mistakes.

Changing the GO TO A-exit to EXIT PARGRAPH/SECTION, or eliminating all
these by using better (IMHO) IF .. ELSE logic and scope terminators
reduces the 'complexity count'  further.
```

On Aug 1, 11:12 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> to say it is all relative and subjective. (Even though you are attempting to

So, do you think that my assigning 10,000 as the complexity factor for
ALTER was not objective ?

I think that is exactly how many times that I would object to an
ALTER.   ;-)


> The fact that the statement COULD be switched from anywhere in the program
> to anywhere else, doesn't mean it WILL be...
>
> And even if it was, statements must appear in source to do it and these can
> be tracked.

Yes, it certainly will be within the source, but that is a point I
have made before.  If, for example, GO TOs are allowed from any point
in a program then the only way to know how a small piece of the
program works is by examining the whole program looking for Go TOs
that are to labels within the range of the code of interest.

If there are ALTERs then this is not enough because each GO TO may
have a range of targets which are not part of the GOTO itself* and
thus for each GOTO the whole program has to be examined for all the
ALTERs which reference this to find out if it could goto the range of
the code of interest.

If we strip off all the ALTERs we reduce by an order of magnitude the
search required to undestand the program.

Similarly with GOTO. If GOTOs do not exist in a program then we can
with greater confidence examine small parts of a program without
needing to understand all of it.  If gotos are only tolerated within a
defined area (such as within each section) then it is only necessary
to examine that to understand enough about the small part in question.

Being lazy I am not interested in examining any more of the source
than is absolutely necessary, so I code to suit that requirement as
best I can.


* I recall (1970?) seeing a program where the altered GO TOs had a
list of possible targets (like a GOTO DEPENDING ON without that
clause) and it would be an error to have an ALTER to one not on that
list. Checking my reference it seems that is not part of COBOL and may
have been an extension. However, I had not seen ALTER before, and once
I worked out what was happening I suggested that the program be burnt
at the stake**, the programmer was not available.

** As a total aside, IIRC in Pocklington, opposite the Black Bull pub,
there is a plaque commenorating the last public witch burning in
england.
```

```


"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:1185941885.429033.151800@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
> On Aug 1, 11:12 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[46 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I have no issue with any of the above.  (Thanks for expressing it clearly 
and succinctly)

However, it is predicated on certain assumptions (not unreasonable ones, but 
ones that most experienced programmers wouldn't do in code we wrote 
ourselves.)

 "If, for example, GO TOs are allowed from any point..." Would you write 
code like that? Do you know of any sites where standards permit code like 
that? So how likely are you to actually encounter code like that, in the 
real world? Yes, I know... you will encounter it occasionally, but it is far 
from the "norm".

There has been a recurrent theme through this thread that just because the 
worst possible scenario COULD happen, it actually WILL. Well, maybe... but 
it certainly won't happen often enough to be a problem. When it does, 
there'll be an update to the site standards :-)

Even when using ALTER, we didn't just allow uncontrolled open slather with 
GO TOs being tweaked all over the place. There has to be some discipline in 
place, otherwise chaos will rule whether you use GO TO, ALTER, SECTIONs or 
anything else...

I actually agree with you on most of the points you've raised. I certainly 
wouldn't argue that using ALTER and GO TO or even SECTIONed code are 
necessarily the BEST ways to go (maybe in certain special cases, as 
mentioned elsewhere), but I just don't feel it so intensly any more. It may 
be because I haven't made my living from COBOL for quite some time now.

And I have never searched a source  program as you describe, so the need to 
do so is foreign to me.  Run a trace, analyse a dump, debug it in animator 
(depending on platform and tools available). It soon reveals what it's 
doing... Interactive debugging is brilliant. I sometimes do it even when I'm 
NOT debugging and the code is performing correctly. It is amazing how 
sometimes you think it is doing something but when you trace it you find it 
isn't, or it is not doing it in the way you thought, or it is doing it 
twice, or a field that you thought was binary, isn't and so on. I'd rather 
spend time watching an actual run than searching through source code. 
(Yesterday this process revealed some stuff about Web Cookies that wasn't 
immediately obvious, but that is another story... :-))

I also tend to keep my SECTIONs small (and distinct :-)), so I simply don't 
have large amounts of source code to pore over. My AVS engine is the largest 
COBOL program I have and that is just over 4000 lines... (Having said that, 
I once maintained a COBOL program with 20,000 lines in it, written in 
Spanish COBOL (I couldn't speak Spanish at the time...;-)). On suggesting 
gently that it might be broken into smaller chunks, the response was: "Why? 
It works correctly..." (it didn't, that was why they had me looking at  it 
:-))

I guess the bottom line is that we all work differently and gradually evolve 
a mode that is "comfortable".

Provided it is effective, I don't see much point in getting excited about 
styles.

Certainly, pointing out pros and cons of approaches is important, especially 
for Newbies, but  beyond that I don't see anything nuch is really gained.


> in a program
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

"Old Wife Greene" in 1630 apparently. (They could've had "Old wife Dashwood" 
for a re-enactment, if I'd known... :-))

GOOGLE says a movie is currently being made...

Pete.

```
On Wed, 1 Aug 2007 18:10:53 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> "If, for example, GO TOs are allowed from any point..." Would you write 
>code like that? Do you know of any sites where standards permit code like 
>that? So how likely are you to actually encounter code like that, in the 
>real world? Yes, I know... you will encounter it occasionally, but it is far 
>from the "norm".

It isn't the conforming, original code that we're worried about.

In the middle of the night emergency maintenance, people write GO TO
PARAGRAPH-EXIT after a cursory check to see what is the actual name of
PARAGRAPH-EXIT.    Sometimes they make mistakes.

If the compiler allows GO TO to anywhere, and the original code was
written with drop-through code, such mistakes happen.

Or they create a paragraph that they perform - and place it at the end
of the program without noticing that there's a performed section above
it.
```

In article <5hcr32F3ia0d6U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]

>As a manager, I would rather manage my user disappointment, than have good

As my Sainted Paternal Grandfather - may he sleep with the angels! - used 
to say, Mr Dashwood, 'Never use yourself as a comparative, you'll only be 
disappointed'.  How you would manage a crew and how other folks manage 
crews just might, possibly, not be the same thing, as recent discussions 
about training have indicated.

>
>As a programmer, I would rather approach problem solving after a good 
>night's sleep, than under pressure in the middle of the night. (I can say 
>this having done both...)

As a consultant/contractor/hired gun I handle it differently.  I make it 
clear that there are two kinds of time, My Time and My Client's Time.  
Sometimes my client gets some of My Time for free... at 2:am when I bolt 
upright in bed and say '*That's* the structure we'll use for that 
subroutine... just like a snake, rolling along with its tail in its 
mouth!' or when a way to solve a problem comes to me as I browse the 
baked-goods section of the supermarket... those I give for free, that's 
part of the way I do business.

When it comes to being on-call, though, my rule is much simpler: can I be 
sloppy-drunk and passed out on my sofa?  If the requirements are such that 
I must maintain acuity or availability then I am not the one dictating 
(within the limits of law) how the time is spent... and if I am not 
dictating how the time is spent then I am on The Company Dime.

This can be dealt with one of two ways... I can be paid a reduced rate for 
the hours that my activities are constrained by the company's expectations 
or I can be paid four hours' full rate per incident, a figure based on the 
provisions of the Fair Labor Standards Act of 1938.

When a client realises that my time costs them money... suddenly I no 
longer am on the Beeper Roster.

>
>I believe that any site where they keep programmers on call 24 hours a day 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>of the 21st century.

Some say that much use of the internal combustion engine falls into that

I was referring to poker, although it might be found in other places... 
sometimes I have heard it called 'over the top' or 'around the bend' or a 
'waterfall'; it is when the sequence of cards contains an ace and ends 
with a lower-value card than it began.

5-6-7-8-9 = 'classic' straight, all numbers in sequence, end higher than 
start

Q-K-A-2-3 = 'kangaroo' straight

DD

On Fri, 3 Aug 2007 16:59:14 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>Maybe that will give you better information than what I'm bandying 
>about... according to http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-standard_poker_hand

>Well, whatever they call it nowadays... it's the bee's knees!

All it does is change the odds from standard poker - similar to the
way wild cards work.   But the odds are kind of odd to begin with,
with straights and flushes not in the order that the rules would
indicate.

But I'm not a gambler (Pure luck games bore me, and I'm a cheapskate),
so I miss out on two interesting games - poker and backgammon (which
is a better game with gambling than without).    I dislike the way the
gambling industry has pre-empted "gaming" to exclude all games except
for their gambling games.

"Serious" poker players can be righteous about the rules.   Even those
who don't know the odds can be there.    But to me, I like big
variations such as the version of poker where you hold your cards so
that everybody can see them except yourself.    This means players
can't just think one way - they have to adjust the way they think.

- **References:** `<5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com> <5h97ekF3hv8npU1@mid.individual.net> <1185913066.280005.293910@i13g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <5h9tuqF3ja9auU1@mid.individual.net> <1185941885.429033.151800@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <5hamffF3i16cfU1@mid.individual.net> <gk81b35uc9u379t8ll1c8bg90ucah196oo@4ax.com> <5hcr32F3ia0d6U1@mid.individual.net> <q9p3b35cjuuit5j5823j13d6i2eoqb44ti@4ax.com> <5hfjm2F3j3nmrU1@mid.individual.net> <svl5b3lhu7k1jfshr1b53umdv25hj234q5@4ax.com>`

```


"Binyamin Dissen" <postingid@dissensoftware.com> wrote in message 
news:svl5b3lhu7k1jfshr1b53umdv25hj234q5@4ax.com...
> On Fri, 3 Aug 2007 14:53:52 +1200 "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Yes, it is. That was never in question. My point is that it is better to get 
a team on the job of fixing it properly, than it is to get one single person 
pulled out of bed in the middle of the night. A delay of a few hours, when 
it means the difference between a considered fix that is less likely to 
cause further problems, and a hasty fix, applied without real consideration 
or analysis of exactly what led to the problem (just, "fix it now") is a 
false economy. From a management point of view, it is simply bad practice.

I would agree, (and have worked in places where it applied) that a software 
failure which stops production is much more serious. However, such places 
normally have rostered  24/7 support BECAUSE a stoppage is so critical. If 
they don't, well, it serves them right and I can't comment further on 
irrelevant bad practice.

As a general rule, amateurs rush in, professionals assess all aspects of a 
situation, balance risk and reward, then pace themselves.
(That means having a clear picture of what you are going to do, before you 
do it)

> Sounds like you have only worked for MF9-5 and have no real concept what
> happens in a 24*7 shop.

Absolutely not true. I have worked in 24/7 services, but that is rostered 
shift work and is not the same thing at all.

In fact, I venture to suggest that I have a better understanding of this 
than you do, judging by your post...

>
> :>(Of course, if one doesn't don't mind being called on, or actually 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> I question what "your watch" is.

If I am a programmer, my "watch" is to provide programming support within 
the times I am rostered to do so. It is implicit that I will ensure I am fit 
for work within those times. (Don't turn up drunk, or drugged, hungover, 
etc...and have enough sleep to render me fit for work.)

If I am a manager, my "watch" is making sure that my resources are not 
overworked or unduly stressed, and that the software I am responsible for is 
maintained in the best possible condition that it can be. It is also part of 
this role to ensure that my clients are managed, and understand what is 
going on and when fixes will be available, and why this may not be 
immediate. (I will have done "groundwork" and will know every client manager 
personally, and will have had lunch with each of them on a regular basis. I 
will be responsive to their needs and treat them like valued customers. When 
something unexpected happens, I will contact them and manage their 
expectations. They know they can trust me to do what's best for all 
concerned, and to ensure a proper solution is provided)

The quality of service is every bit as important as the quantity of it. If 
it takes a few more hours to provide a quality service, then that is what 
we'll do, UNLESS someone is going to die if we do not address the problem 
immediately.

I have worked like this for nearly 20 years now, and never had a problem 
with Users or Client managers, finding that, when they are kept informed, 
they have no problem with unexpected events. I have dealt with bullying 
senior managers who demand that "something" be done "right now",  told them 
it would be managed, addressed and fixed, and that they could hold me 
responsible if it wasn't,  then actually had them apologise later, after 
talking to the clients.

I have never been fired for failing to address a problem in the middle of 
the night, or refusing to do as ordered by some senior manager, when it 
simply didn't make sense. (I have upset a few people on occasion, but you 
can't make an omelette without breaking eggs...)

I'm not looking for work and have no trouble getting it when I want it, so I 
must be doing SOMETHING right... :-)

It is OK for managers to be called in the middle of night (that goes with 
the territory), it is NOT OK for IT staff to be, and if anyone called my 
staff without calling me first (and  anyone who has ever worked for me knows 
they don't answer to anyone else), there would be confrontation in the 
morning.

Knee jerk reactions to unexpected failures (like pulling people out of bed 
in the middle of the night)  are NOT a part of quality management.

Pete.

"I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

We took the philosophy of fixing it now and for all time where it was
possible to do so. Other teams took the false economy of bye-pass now

problems. They are also much more amenable to having their problems worked

us to sort out problems. When they are working with you and understand what 
is involved they are much more reasonable in their demands.

perception is everything. I s'pose I should add that I pay for these lunches 
out of my own pocket. In companies that are very tight fisted, people 
appreciate being taken to lunch.

Let me recap this subthread:

1.  If a program works, it shouldn't matter what style it is coded in.

2.  I prefer a style that makes maintenance in the middle of the night
easier.

3.  Maintenance in the middle of the night is sooo 20th century.

4.   But it happens anyway.

5.   It shouldn't.


So the question is - is easy-to-maintain code irrelevant when we
shouldn't need to do such maintenance?

```
In article <68h6b39sd9vq8dq38o4lmdoholbinbar78@4ax.com>, Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
>Let me recap this subthread:
>
>1.  If a program works, it shouldn't matter what style it is coded in.

I disagree, due to the reason cited immediately below (and others).
>
>2.  I prefer a style that makes maintenance in the middle of the night
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>5.   It shouldn't.

"God grant me the courage to change the things I can, the serenity to accept 
the things I cannot, and the wisdom to know the difference."
>
>
>So the question is - is easy-to-maintain code irrelevant when we
>shouldn't need to do such maintenance?

No, it's not irrelevant at all -- because we do routine, as well as emergency, 
maintenance. Effective management may perhaps eliminate the need for emergency 
middle-of-the-night maintenance, except in truly critical circumstances, but 
the need for routine maintenance will never go away. Business requirements 
expand and change continually. Maintenance will always be necessary. Thus 
easy-to-maintain code is always important.
```

```
In article <5hi59rF3j0dmnU1@mid.individual.net>, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>(I have components I have never maintained, and some of them I couldn't, 
>even if I wanted to, as I don't have the source. It isn't a problem, despite 
>changes to the systems they are in.)

Patch the object... <g>

Last time I did that was January 2000 -- had a program with "19" hard-coded as 
the century. Program was written in Cobol-74. Site no longer had a license for 
the Cobol-74 compiler. Program made external calls that required the Cobol-74 
run time library, so it couldn't be simply recompiled in Cobol-85. Options 
were to spend several thousand dollars on a license to compile one program one 
time (government agency - ain't gonna happen), find somebody with a system 
that did have the -74 compiler and port the source over there to be compiled 
(agency was specifically a classified Defense Dept. site - REALLY ain't gonna 
happen), spend a week rewriting to use the Cobol-85 RTL (and spend another two 
weeks revalidating - that ain't gonna happen either) -- or spend five minutes 
patching the object.

I don't think I ever did tell the PHB precisely quite *how* I fixed it. <g>
```

Don't think I wouldn't/haven't :-)

It was standard practice on the old NCR 315. A COBOL  compile took around 45 
minutes so it was a lot easier to simply patch the object deck... (yes, they 
were punch cards :-))  We used to patch object decks, keep track of the 
patches and the source code needed togenerate them (kind of like reverse 
engineering COBOL code :-)) then do a compile at the end of the week to 
bring the code back in line with the object...

Ah, happy times... :-)

…[21 more quoted lines elided]…

```


"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:68h6b39sd9vq8dq38o4lmdoholbinbar78@4ax.com...
> Let me recap this subthread:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> easier.

Why qualify it with "middle of the night"? Why not simply: "I prefer a style 
that makes maintenance easier."?


>
> 3.  Maintenance in the middle of the night is sooo 20th century.

Yes, it is. But that's not why I'm against it... :-)

>
> 4.   But it happens anyway.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> shouldn't need to do such maintenance?

It's a very good and succinct summary, Howard. (Maybe we could use more of 
these in CLC?)

My final position and answer to your question:

1. We should be striving to eliminate maintenance.
2. We also have to deal with reality, and reality is that maintenance is 
(currently) required.
3.  Therefore, easy-to-maintain code is to be preferred over the 
alternatives, given that the code works.

The debatable parts are what constitutes "easy-to-maintain" (this tends to 
be subjective) , whether or not dragging people out of bed to do maintenance 
improves the overall maintainability of the code long term, and what 
responsibility management should take for the state of code, even though 
they may not be directly responsible for writing it.

I have tried to offer some positive suggestions, based on my own experience, 
for addressing all of these things.

It isn't just about programmers and managers. It is about IT as a whole 
(both of these parties) working to provide a service to clients and making 
it a service we can all be proud of.

It is far too easy to sit back and say: "Well, if we didn't have such crap 
management we could achieve so much more" Or "If our programmers weren't so 
incompetent we'd have much better software" or, "Well, I did everything 
right, it was my stupid manager who screwed us".

Just whingeing about it achieves nothing.

I'd really like to see positive statements here about what CAN be done in 
these scenarios.

(and anyone who says "nothing" should think again about their attitude and 
especially, their level of imagination and creativity.)

People who are bright enough to produce solutions to comlex business 
problems, should be able to deal with poor management, poor colleagues, and 
poor workplaces.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ALTER design (Was: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through)

_(reply depth: 25)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2007-08-04T12:23:00+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f91r33$320$1@reader2.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<5h97ekF3hv8npU1@mid.individual.net> <5hglvqF3k2o1hU1@mid.individual.net> <68h6b39sd9vq8dq38o4lmdoholbinbar78@4ax.com> <5hi4rsF3jpgvpU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <5hi4rsF3jpgvpU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]

>I have tried to offer some positive suggestions, based on my own experience, 
>for addressing all of these things.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>Just whingeing about it achieves nothing.

Just whingeing about a situation, Mr Dashwood, might do less overall harm 
or equal good as an 'all ya gotta do' solution which ignores the 
deeply-rooted causes.  When approaching what appears to be the 
skin-blemish of 'our programmers get woken up in the middles of the night' 
close enough to see that it - in some cases - the surfacing of a cancerous 
kind of organisational behavior for rewards and punishments...

... and then to say 'all we gotta to is rouge the blemish' is, in my 
opinion, facile and demeaning.  The depth, extent and quality of the 
corruption of the flesh must be first determined and then acknowledged... 
first in private, of course, so that friction caused by embarassments are 
minimised... but eventually it will become public.

What I try to do, Mr Dashwood, is encourage that kind of examination... 
and it is a rather simple thing to do.  I base it on the assumption that 
individuals (except for ones who are best tended to by The Professionals) 
will tend to do things they see as being in the best interests of 
themselves and those around them... if this were not the case then the 
species, being a social one, would not survive.

So... if things are going badly and, by definition, people will tend to do 
things they see as being in the best interests of themselves and those 
around them... next comes the question from that paradigmmatic 
Organisation State: Qui bono?  It takes effort to make a structure for 
activity and it takes effort to maintain it (if only in the face of 
entropy)... who sees benefit from the structure?

There are many different kinds of benefit, of course... and that is where 
facile 'all ya gotta do is' solutions fall apart.  If the benefit is one 
of short-term financial gain then the solution must provide either more 
short-term gain or change a person's outlook - not a simple task - to 
favor something else over short-term gain.

If the benefit is one of status then the solution is even more difficult; 
it requires the changing of many people's behavior to grant more status 
for the new behavior than the old.

If the benefit is one of avoiding work then the solution is slightly 
easier; it requires restructuring of the work environment so that the old 
behavior requires So Much More work that the new one is adopted.

Many, many years ago - before people were talking about it - I read Sun 
T'zu's 'The Art of War'.  One thing that struck me was a side-note of how 
The General had to appeal to different kinds of people in different ways; 
motivate the greedy by appealing to their desire for gain, motivate the 
intelligent by appealing to their desire to demonstrate superiority, 
motivate the lazy by appealing to their desire for sloth... there was no 
'one size fits all' solution for any group of people, let alone for a 
problem which requires the co-operation of various groups - in this case 
programmers, managers, executives and customers - to solve.

So, Mr Dashwood, to answer a question of yours from a bit back in the 
thread - how would I deal with an organisation's (long-established 
situation)  that causes (discomfort for some folks) - is easy; all I gotta 
do is use my position of responsibility and authority in the organisation 
to set things up so that those who see themselves as benefitting from the 
way things are now will see themselves as benefitting more from doing 
things a different way.

DD
```

A big example of this type of questioning is the whole environment of

should not be written down.    This has Mr. Spock style irrationality

```


"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:u4feb3tecmn2i2bs6kjjfc4puhn42i850b@4ax.com...
> On Sat, 4 Aug 2007 13:59:23 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> should not be written down.

I was on a site that got audited while I was there and they came up with 
exactly this. It was just crazy to expect that people (being "people") will 
implement a whole bunch of security measures that involve considerable extra 
effort and inconvenience, when there had been no security breaches. (Even 
though, logically, they should.)


>   This has Mr. Spock style irrationality
> requiring that people don't act like people.

Exactly.

> We all know that this
> system doesn't work - and we are unwilling to spend the money on
> biometric replacements.    How do we get from where we are now, to
> where we need to be without paying for the journey?
>

Not quite sure what you mean here, Howard.

The way I dealt with this was to get the whole team around a table and 
discuss and agree that we would change passwords regularly (and use 
meaningless ones). We also decided that rather than leaving passwords on 
post-its :-) we would create a database with entries for every member of the 
team on our central server, encrypt it, and make sure that access to it was 
only available by authorization from me. This meant if anyone needed to 
change their password, look at their password, or know someone else's 
password, it had to go through me, and I would issue them with a random 
password to grant "one-time" access to our "passwords" DB.

The Auditors were very impressed... but, in practice, people simply told 
each other passwords if a team member needed to access something of 
theirs... :-) (Of course, if you forgot your OWN passwords, then you had to 
use the system... :-))

There is apparency, and there is reality, and into that you must mix the 
nature of Human Beings :-)

It is fair to say that our security was never compromised, despite the 
"unoffical" routes taken through the security requirements.


> I suppose we hope our competitors pay for the journey, and we can ride
> their shirt tails.   I don't think that will work.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> wrong place.

Agreed.

Pete
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> So the question is - is easy-to-maintain code irrelevant when we
> shouldn't need to do such maintenance?

Depending on how the code is structured, the "shouldn't" need to do 
maintenance angle assumes that the world stays the same.  (I know CBSE* 
is supposed to eliminate most of this, but so few places do it right, 
it's never going to become reality - or at least not in my lifetime.)

I've been really impressed with the way our current system is 
structured.  We have objects and services (among other things).  The 
objects have two constructors - one that returns a new, empty one, and 
one that, given a "ReturnObject" (a row of data from the database, 
basically), populates its properties.  If an object needs more or fewer 
properties, as long as the creating service and the constructor know 
about them, nothing else even cares!

It's so easy to make changes.  Granted, this separation means that some 
changes have to be made across several files, but it's easy to make 
isolated yet wide-spread changes at the same time.  :)

So no, easy-to-maintain code isn't irrelevant, and won't be for a long 
while to come.
```

```


"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:1186147240.523141.140380@k79g2000hse.googlegroups.com...
> On 3 Aug, 03:53, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
> wrote:
…[58 more quoted lines elided]…
> determined client from their requirements.

No, but they can insist that these "requirements" be explained and 
justified...


> Incidentally, some of the
> clients that I have worked for seem, to me, to be the kind of people
> capable of homicide, so to not provide callout could be deemed to be
> life-threatening.
>
Fair comment :-) (Some clients have thought that about me...:-)).

A well managed site, recognising that their clients require 24/7 service, 
would roster shifts and charge the clients for it.

A wise, well managed site,  would point out that the added expense would not 
be necessary if they were prepared to wait a few hours. It would then be up 
to the clients to show how waiting a few hours was unacceptable, and they 
would have to pay. Maybe a compromise where one staff member (rostered) was 
on call (and the implications of this on development and maintenance 
projects were made clear to the clients). With the implications fully 
explored by all parties, a call can be made that is acceptable to all 
concerned.

(Maybe the IT department need to employ more staff, maybe user departments 
need to realise that even though they think something is critical, it may 
not be. What if the whole system was off the air for 7 or 8 hours? 
(earthquake, terrorist attack...???)I have been involved in Disaster 
Recovery planning with user departments who, at first dismayed by the time 
lag before the backup system came on line, after calming down and looking at 
it, realised it really wasn't so bad.)

Every scemario is different, but the underlying principle is to work 
together to get a solution acceptable to all affected parties.

I still believe that midnight callouts for program maintenance are just 
foolish, for all concerned.

Pete.
```

```
On 3 Aug, 15:06, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

> > Surely, (I must stop calling you that), callout is either a value
> > added service offered (which could be unnecessary if not for life
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> justified...

I want. How much more justification do they need?

>
>
> A well managed site, recognising that their clients require 24/7 service,
> would roster shifts and charge the clients for it.

24/7 shift patterns would be overkill where only one system has
problems and that is usually Monday night. Callout was the appropriate
cost-effective response which produced quality fixes. Unfortunately,
as the client was charged for two days per week support as part of the
total package, he never saw the monetary cost of the systems falling
over. If each abend had been charged separately he would have seen the
true cost of development (and probably complained about excessive
chargesm, as he was wont to do).

>
> A wise, well managed site,  would point out that the added expense would not
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> concerned.

That was how we had operated but then the Big Boss (COI) decided to
split the teams out into development and a dedicated support team and
that is when quality dropped. Development managers never found out how
bad their developers were (unless those annoying support people came
up and disturbed their staff asking for advice and demanding fixes)
and their staff never learned from their mistakes.

>
> (Maybe the IT department need to employ more staff,

More like the IT department should employ cheaper staff, because we
were costing the users too much. The cost and time delays from any
request were definitely used as excuses to migrate to AS/400s and
packaged solutions.


> maybe user departments
> need to realise that even though they think something is critical, it may
> not be.

Agreed. Unfortunately, many managers will bend over backwards to
satisfy clients because they are afraid of losing clients. I developed
a system that took three weeks to develop. We were tasked to develop
it by the manager because the client had said that he could write the
two programs at home on his ICL DRS in a weekend and we were required
to do the job in three days. Impossible, I responded. The manager
looked at my estimate and asked: why do we need to do any analysis
(that saves a week) and we won't need to test (so that is another week
saved) and you can deliver by Friday or do you want me to go back to
the client and tell him that we can't do it because he'll go
elsewhere?

Three weeks later we were still working on it. I only finished the
last spec on the Friday. And it was 9 programs not two.
```

On Aug 4, 2:06 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> I still believe that midnight callouts for program maintenance are just
> foolish, for all concerned.

I used to do work part of the time for a small UK consultancy by
developing a system which I had originally written and they supported
for the UK and worldwide users. During development periods they would
test during their daytime office hours and send all the details back
to me, plus new requirements, so that I could revise during their
overnight.

It worked out quite well but occasionally they would want to
communicate by voice and/or get a fix so they could complete some
testing.

I also spent a dozen or so months in the UK over a few years when they
paid me to do development work 'on-site' (plus travel and
accommodation).

Amusingly I am writing at this time because I just got a call at 3am
to fix stuff (which I did while still on the phone). But I knew it
could happen because the client is changing over a system and 'the
management' is in the office and had arranged for me to be available
at this time.

```
On 2 Aug, 02:41, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:
> --
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything.""Howard Brazee" <how...@brazee.net> wrote in message
…[66 more quoted lines elided]…
> - Show quoted text -

As one who has been called out repeatedly overnight (same suite, same
job, same program, same error - abend due to user data input causing
excessive levels of error) I can honestly say:

1.  I have only ever implemented one fix that has subsequently been
seen to be at fault - it was an inefficient blocksize on a tape;

2.  Apart from (1), and the programmer who inadvertently wiped the
entire weeks' data from an input file by restoring the wrong gdg, I am
not aware of any other overnight fixes that were subsequently deemed
to be faulty;

3.  I have worked with many people, of various skill levels, and with
the proviso of (2) they have implemented hundreds of overnight fixes
that have been deemed to be correct and permanent solutions to the
problems;

4.  I am very *issed off that the quality of systems moving from
development into operational status (particularly where the move
requires transfering responsibility across teams) is such that the
errors arising from poor developments far outways the number of errors
arising from faulty fixes (and that includes 3 am fixes);

5.  I do not know of any manager who is adept at handling the users'
disappointment nor do I know of any user who is happy with a 10
million pound invoicing run failing at 2 am and not being fixed or
rerun because the programmer wanted a good nights sleep;

6.  I know of many programmers, myself included, who prefer a good
nights sleep rather than repeated call outs (so we agree on that one).
I gave up doing the callout because the money did not compensate for
losing a nights sleep;

7.  The quality of delivered systems improved, where I worked, when I
became active in the handover process and started looking at design,
code and test documents and insisted upon the developers providing the
first three months' standby cover.

What is it that history teaches us? Those who fail to study history
are doomed to repeat the errors and those who study history are doomed
to repeat the errors endlessly?
```
