```
Coder: 'Boss, (x) is giving us trouble again... me, Mary and Jim have all 
been woken up at 2:am to deal with it.  I've scoped out the code and I 
think it'll take me about two days of dedicated time to straighten it 
out.'

Boss: 'There's no change req for this... so your time and the cost of 
testing will come out of my budget.  Leave it alone.'

Coder: 'But that means the on-call programmer will most likely get woken 
up again!'

Boss (said): 'Don't worry, we'll clean up the edits and it won't happen 
again... now, can you get those ad-hoc reports out to Rosotti in the 
Corner Office before noon?'

Boss (unsaid): 'When the code blows up in the middle of the night the time 
you spend to fix it becomes part of the run's cost and I get to charge 
(department) for it; this helps in showing my department as a provider of 
valuable services and increases my raise/bonus.'

to remember that there might be just as many folks willing to *take* any 
given job as there are folks willing to leave it.  *You* may see a 
'Jobsworth' who just goes along to get along, others might see a person 
with a spouse and children who's trying to pay the rent in as timely a 
manner as possible by performing a legally-permitted task.

... and here's another: someone with sufficient authority saw it as being
```

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

```
Granted that the 'throw a pot of money at the problem' does not always 
produce less bug-ridden systems... but a solid (non-moving) goal, thorough 
analysis, in-depth code review and rigorous testing tend to, in my 
experience, produce fewer systems where 'hundreds of overnight fixes' are 
required.

I know very few programmers who say, in all seriousness, 'we can get the 
job done without analysis' or 'we can get the job done without reviewing 
the code' or 'we can get the job done without rigorous testing'... and I

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>on the job and he was one of the better staff).

Ahhhhh, a classic... I was taught, e'er-so-long ago, that comparisons were 
much cheaper than other operations, hence you'll find my code riddled with 
things like

If Input-Date NOT = Prev-Date
    Perform Date-Ritual

... or ...

If Input-Client-No NOT = Prev-Client-No
    Perform Read-Client-Master

... and the like.  To put your snoozy colleague into the framework I 
established above - 

Which analyst wrote the specs which required validation of identical 
dates?

Who sat in on the code reviews and neglected to point out the waste of 
machine time?

What kind of volume-testing was done to show the amount of machine-time 
used by the method was unacceptable?

The coder, while a ready target for a pointed finger, did not work in a 
vacuum.  If the organisation is rotten then the code is more likely to be 
rotten; people tend to reflect the expectations of the institutions with 
which they choose to involved themselves... or so those pesky Sociologists 
have said.

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

>implementation dates agreed before anyone has a clear idea of the

'On time, on spec or on budget: pick two'... and that's not original, at 
all.  To establish an implementation date means that there will be an 
allocation, co-ordination and motivation of personnel and resources 
towards the accomplishment of a stated Executive goal... I believe I've 
stated what the textbooks I have read have had to say about whose job that 
is.

>Also, years of
>experience allows us to pre-empt many possible problems and design/
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ALTER design (Was: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through)

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2007-08-04T12:36:26+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f91rsa$l36$1@reader2.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <f8vrpd$16a$1@reader2.panix.com> <5hi753F3l6822U1@mid.individual.net> <1186228415.430392.324250@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>`

```
In article <1186228415.430392.324250@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com>,
Alistair  <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>On 4 Aug, 03:38, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
>wrote:
>> <docdw...@panix.com> wrote in messagenews:f8vrpd$16a$1@reader2.panix.com...

[snip]

>> > I don't know many Web programmers, true... but if I hear anyone tell me
>> > that they can get a programming-task accomplished 'without rigorous
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>> sites without rigourous testing is either very foolish or very lucky.

My apologies, Mr Maclean, for being obscure; your interpretation is 
close... but not complete.  The example at hand is one of programming but 
the principle is a bit more broad and might be generalised as the 
exchange:

A: 'This solution to the problem is right.'

B  'Oh, really... according to what?'

The 'according to what' can be many things... system tests, navigation 
charts, tables of height/weight restrictions, etc... but solutions do not 
exist in vacuo.  Consider:

A: 'The solution to 'What does 1 + 1 equal?' is '2'.'

B: 'That is wrong.  1 + 1 = 10.'

One is right according to the rules of trinary-or-greater notation, the 
other is right according to the rules of binary notation.

So... the assertion of 'This is right' merits, I would say, at least a 
minimal consideration of 'According to what?'.

DD
```

```
On 3 Aug, 18:29, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:

> Granted that the 'throw a pot of money at the problem' does not always
> produce less bug-ridden systems... but a solid (non-moving) goal, thorough
> analysis, in-depth code review and rigorous testing tend to, in my
> experience, produce fewer systems where 'hundreds of overnight fixes' are
> required.

Years ago I read an article which said that 90% of IT projects would
benefit by having the implementation delayed by one month. I fail to
understand why managers would rather implement dubious quality rather
than put back an arbitrary implementation date. I can understand the
desire to not have the budget inflated by one months' salaries and
there is such a thing as losing face but implementing quality saves
money in the long run (contentious statement with no hard evidence to
back it up).

>
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

There is a school of thought that would have NOT abolished. Perhaps
your code would read better as:

If Input-Date = Prev-Date
    blah blah blah
else
    Perform Date-Ritual
end-if.



> ... and the like.  To put your snoozy colleague into the framework I
> established above -
>
> Which analyst wrote the specs which required validation of identical
> dates?

Definitely not me. The spec probably said that all dates should be
validated and left the detail to the snoozy programmer (who is not my
boozy chum).

>
> Who sat in on the code reviews and neglected to point out the waste of
> machine time?

Code reviews? If it worked on one record in test then it was probably
passed as is.

>
> What kind of volume-testing was done to show the amount of machine-time
> used by the method was unacceptable?

None. I only found the error when we upgraded from Natural 2.2.3 to
Natural 2.3. The upgrade penalised calls to other Natural programs and
to Cobol programs. Someone in ops ran a report monthly which noticed
the sudden increase in cpu time (one program went from a nundred cpu
seconds to over 2000 cpu seconds). I was tasked with investigating
why. DBA said that there was nothing wrong with the way the language
and database (Adabas) were running. In investigating the worst program
I was able to save about 75% of the excess cpu by obviating repeated
validation (as per your method) but that did not return the usage to
the original levels.

>
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> I think I smell an 'Ahhhhhh, for the Oldene Dayse' there, Mr Maclean.

Yes indeed. Standards are in decline.

>
```

```
>your code would read better as:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>end-if.

Aaah, but what if "blah blah blah" is nothing at all? Eliminating NOT would 
require replacing constructs such as

IF Input-Date NOT = Prev-Date
        PERFORM Date-Ritual
END-IF

with

IF Input-Date = Prev-Date
        CONTINUE
ELSE
        PERFORM Date-Ritual
END-IF

which is IMO a *far* worse abomination than the use of NOT.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<J32ti.630$3x.597@newssvr25.news.prodigy.net>`
- **References:** `<13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5hflj7F3kpge5U1@mid.individual.net> <f8v5lr$ksh$1@reader2.panix.com> <1186151221.693030.236290@b79g2000hse.googlegroups.com> <f8vomd$e38$1@reader2.panix.com> <1186191243.655094.162380@r34g2000hsd.googlegroups.com> <iYQsi.55565$5j1.10088@newssvr21.news.prodigy.net> <1186227636.237724.32460@o61g2000hsh.googlegroups.com>`

```
In article <1186227636.237724.32460@o61g2000hsh.googlegroups.com>, Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:

>I am happy with NOT being used to simplify constructs. 

Same here.

>However, I have
>met programmers who would have difficulty understanding a moderate
>level of complex IF using NOT. 

Yes, I have too -- and I've always thought that such folk were in the wrong 
line of work.

>I am also happy with CONTINUE and the
>older NEXT SENTENCE.

I prefer to use constructs that make them unnecessary, e.g.

IF NOT <condition>
        <statement>
END-IF

instead of 

IF <condition>
        CONTINUE
ELSE
        <statement>
END-IF

which IMO unnecessarily impedes understanding the function of the program, 
while contributing no compensating advantages whatever. But the one that 
*really* drives me up the wall is

IF NOT <condition>
        NEXT SENTENCE
ELSE
        <statement> .

That, to me, is a complete inversion of the natural way of considering a 
test-and-action sequence, and should always be written as the far simpler and 
more understandable

IF <condition>
        <statement> .
```

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:5hkgiaF3k84enU1@mid.individual.net...
>
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
[snip]
> >>However, I have
> >>met programmers who would have difficulty understanding a moderate
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Yep. Me too...

Perhaps with such weaknesses as maintenance programmers,
their new line of work should be new program development.
In that position, they might keep the knots, ... er NOTs, out
of future code. <g>

Seriously, consider the problem of general exclusionary rules.
Such rules state, in effect, that a particular action is not to be
taken; but those rules, over time, may come to have exceptions.
From a program maintenance standpoint, having somewhere to
place those exceptions seems worthwhile.

    if general-exclusionary-rule
        continue
    else
        perform default-action
    end-if

The 'continue' statement is the placeholder for the inevitable
exceptions to the general rule. When exceptions need to be
added to the code, the 'continue' is replaced with the necessary
tests.

        perform tests-for-exception
        if exception-to-general-rule
            perform default-action
        end-if

If the original code is written with a 'not',

    if not general-exclusionary-rule
        perform default-action
    end-if

The tendency might be to begin stacking one exception after
another into the 'if' condition; thus leading to a maintenance
nightmare.

    if (not general-exclusionary-rule)
      or (1st-exception)
      or (2d-exception)
      or (...)
      or (nth-exception)
        perform default-action
    end-if

In this case, one might say (and I will): When a general
exclusionary rule is present, use 'continue' instead of 'not'
as an exception to the general exclusionary rule, "Use 'not'
instead of 'continue'". <g>
```

```
In article <1186273558.137520.199470@l70g2000hse.googlegroups.com>, Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:

>Whereas I agree with you about retaining and using NOT rather than
>using inelegant IF NOT...ELSE constructs I have found that inserting
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>seen run over four pages long.

In the construct

IF <condition>
        <statement>
ELSE
        CONTINUE
END-IF

ELSE CONTINUE (or ELSE NEXT SENTENCE, as the case may be) is redundant, and 
can easily be omitted with no loss of clarity. 

Conversely, in the construct

IF <condition>
        CONTINUE
ELSE
        <statement>
END-IF

CONTINUE ELSE can (and IMO should) be omitted simply by inverting <condition> 
to NOT <condition>  -- with a considerable *gain* in clarity by so doing.
```

```
>>> On 8/4/2007 at 7:21 PM, in message
<DC9ti.26508$RX.17910@newssvr11.news.prodigy.net>, Doug
Miller<spambait@milmac.com> wrote:
> In article <1186273558.137520.199470@l70g2000hse.googlegroups.com>, 
> Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> to NOT <condition>  -- with a considerable *gain* in clarity by so doing.


Something I find interesting about Ruby, though perhaps a bit problematic,
is what they call a 'statement modifier'.  (I think this is inherited from
Perl.)

In general, you code an if statement in the obvious way:

if condition
    puts 'something'
    do_something_else()
end

If, however, you want to do one and only one thing if the condition is true
you can do it like this:

do_something() if condition

Which does in one line the same as would require three lines with a regular
if statement:

if condition
    do_something()
end

Of course you could still do one line like this:
if condition then do_something() end

(note required 'then')
But I don't care for the placement of the 'end'.

And, as I mentioned in a previous, there is also "unless", which functions
as "if not".

do_something() unless condition

Statement modifiers don't allow for else (at least I don't think they do!). 
So if you ended up needing one at a later point you'd end up rewriting it as
a regular if/else.  Not sure if this is good or bad.  I haven't done enough
"real" coding in Ruby to say whether I like this or not, but it seems a
fairly interesting construct.

Frank
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ALTER design (Was: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through)

_(reply depth: 30)_

- **From:** Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2007-08-06T16:43:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
On Mon, 06 Aug 2007 16:25:20 GMT, spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller)
wrote:

>>IF  CONDITION
>>     CONTINUE
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>to understand.

I don't agree that it is easier to understand, but here's an example
of what happens once we have maintained these using the minimal change
mandate of maintenance programming:

IF  CONDITION
     PERFORM MY-NEW-PARAGRAPH
ELSE
     PERFORM MY-PARAGRAPH
END-IF

is easy to maintain.
IF NOT CONDITION
        PERFORM MY-PARAGRAPH
ELSE
        PERFORM MY-NEW-PARAGRAPH.
END-IF
```

- **Date:** 2007-08-06T13:38:58-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<46B72472.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<1186151221.693030.236290@b79g2000hse.googlegroups.com> <f8vomd$e38$1@reader2.panix.com> <1186191243.655094.162380@r34g2000hsd.googlegroups.com> <iYQsi.55565$5j1.10088@newssvr21.news.prodigy.net> <1186227636.237724.32460@o61g2000hsh.googlegroups.com> <J32ti.630$3x.597@newssvr25.news.prodigy.net> <1186273558.137520.199470@l70g2000hse.googlegroups.com> <odgeb3ltb7u7449peglr732tvj8kb430gb@4ax.com> <QXHti.47481$YL5.40752@newssvr29.news.prodigy.net> <jajeb3tkl7rc98u4fki3o3kg67hscovi3g@4ax.com>`

```
>>> On 8/6/2007 at 10:35 AM, in message
<jajeb3tkl7rc98u4fki3o3kg67hscovi3g@4ax.com>, Howard
Brazee<howard@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Mon, 06 Aug 2007 16:25:20 GMT, spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller)
> wrote:
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> END-IF

I'm kind of a fan of Ruby's "unless" expression.  (I'm sure other languages
have it as well:

unless condition
    do_something()
end

Getting back to COBOL, I wonder what others think about this:

EVALUATE CONDITION
WHEN FALSE
    PERFORM MY-PARAGRAPH
END-EVALUATE

The maintenance programmer could then make the following change.

EVALUATE CONDITION
WHEN FALSE
    PERFORM MY-PARAGRAPH
WHEN TRUE
    PERFORM MY-NEW-PARAGRAPH
END-EVALUATE

or

EVALUATE CONDITION
WHEN TRUE
    PERFORM MY-NEW-PARAGRAPH
WHEN FALSE
    PERFORM MY-PARAGRAPH
END-EVALUATE

...depending on your preference.

I've never actually used it, until I tried it just now, but it seems pretty
straight forward, easy to understand and easy to maintain.  I just may start
using it!

In some ways EVALUATE simply makes IF redundant.  The only thing IF saves
you is a small amount of typing.

Speaking of EVALUATE versus IF, I just happened to be working on something a
few minutes ago that got me thinking...  What is easier to read:

IF (WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NUMERIC       
       AND CL-FIELD-6 NUMERIC                     
       AND WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) = CL-FIELD-6)  
OR (WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NOT NUMERIC   
       AND CL-FIELD-6 (1:17) = '*****************')
    PERFORM 0222-UPDATE-WIRELOG-FILE        
ELSE
    STRING 'WIRX - OLD LOG NBR '            
           CL-FIELD-5                       
           ' DOES NOT MATCH YOUR INPUT '    
           CL-FIELD-6                       
      DELIMITED BY SIZE                     
      INTO MSG-DISPLAY                      
    END-STRING                              
END-IF

or

EVALUATE TRUE                               
WHEN WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NUMERIC       
 AND CL-FIELD-6 NUMERIC                     
 AND WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) = CL-FIELD-6  
WHEN WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NOT NUMERIC   
 AND CL-FIELD-6 (1:17) = '*****************'
    PERFORM 0222-UPDATE-WIRELOG-FILE        
WHEN OTHER                                  
    STRING 'WIRX - OLD LOG NBR '            
           CL-FIELD-5                       
           ' DOES NOT MATCH YOUR INPUT '    
           CL-FIELD-6                       
      DELIMITED BY SIZE                     
      INTO MSG-DISPLAY                      
    END-STRING                              
END-EVALUATE                                

I'm just about to implement the latter.  One thing I like about it is you
don't (in this case) need parentheses.  The original code looked like this:

IF WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NUMERIC       
AND CL-FIELD-6 NUMERIC                     
AND WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) = CL-FIELD-6
    PERFORM 0222-UPDATE-WIRELOG-FILE        
ELSE
    STRING 'WIRX - OLD LOG NBR '            
           CL-FIELD-5                       
           ' DOES NOT MATCH YOUR INPUT '    
           CL-FIELD-6                       
      DELIMITED BY SIZE                     
      INTO MSG-DISPLAY                      
    END-STRING                              
END-IF

So I had the choice of doing either the IF statement with the parentheses or
the EVALUATE.  I chose EVALUATE.

New ideas every day!

Frank
```

```
>>> On 8/6/2007 at 5:21 PM, in message
<h3Oti.2123$jO3.1575@nlpi070.nbdc.sbc.com>, Doug
Miller<spambait@milmac.com> wrote:
> In article <46B72472.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>, "Frank Swarbrick" 
> <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote:
…[49 more quoted lines elided]…
> More straightforward than IF-ELSE? Not IMO.

I didn't say "more straightforward".  Just "straightforward".  :-)
 
> EVALUATE is *great* for selection among multiple alternatives, but for a 
> 
> simple either-or, I find IF-ELSE preferable.

I don't disagree necessarily.  I was just interested in exploring the
options.  One of the things I like about the EVALUATE here, even when it's
just a choice between two alternatives, is that you need not *change* any
lines when adding the alternative.  In your example you had to change from
"IF NOT CONDITION" to "IF CONDITION".  Big deal?  No, not really.  But, as I
mentioned in a previous message, I prefer not to change lines where they
"aren't really changing".

Will I ever really use "WHEN FALSE" and "WHEN TRUE" instead of IF/ELSE? 
Maybe not.  Or maybe.  Depends on how I feel next time it comes up!  :-)

>>
>>In some ways EVALUATE simply makes IF redundant.  The only thing IF saves
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> further improve readability IMO.

I would accept this indentation to make it more readable

EVALUATE TRUE                               

WHEN WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NUMERIC       
         AND CL-FIELD-6 NUMERIC                     
         AND WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) = CL-FIELD-6  

WHEN WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NOT NUMERIC   
         AND CL-FIELD-6 (1:17) = '*****************'

    PERFORM 0222-UPDATE-WIRELOG-FILE        

WHEN OTHER                                  

    STRING 'WIRX - OLD LOG NBR '            
           CL-FIELD-5                       
           ' DOES NOT MATCH YOUR INPUT '    
           CL-FIELD-6                       
      DELIMITED BY SIZE                     
      INTO MSG-DISPLAY                      
    END-STRING                              

END-EVALUATE                                

But I wouldn't indent the actually conditionally executed statements any
further, for reasons I've given already.

Actually, just the addition of some blank lines makes it fairly readable.


EVALUATE TRUE                               

WHEN WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NUMERIC       
 AND CL-FIELD-6 NUMERIC                     
 AND WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) = CL-FIELD-6  

WHEN WIRELOG-NBR (CL-FIELD-5) NOT NUMERIC   
 AND CL-FIELD-6 (1:17) = '*****************'

    PERFORM 0222-UPDATE-WIRELOG-FILE        

WHEN OTHER                                  

    STRING 'WIRX - OLD LOG NBR '            
           CL-FIELD-5                       
           ' DOES NOT MATCH YOUR INPUT '    
           CL-FIELD-6                       
      DELIMITED BY SIZE                     
      INTO MSG-DISPLAY                      
    END-STRING                              

END-EVALUATE                                

Too late now, since I already turned it in, but perhaps in the future!

Frank
```

```
n 8/6/2007 at 6:26 PM, in message
```

```
On Aug 7, 4:23 am, spamb...@milmac.com (Doug Miller) wrote:
> In article <k8geb3djttctum50sjf8d1m55531hml...@4ax.com>, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> >On Sat, 04 Aug 2007 01:51:10 GMT, spamb...@milmac.com (Doug Miller)
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Because it inverts the natural way of thinking about a test-action sequence

It inverts _your_ way of thinking about ...

It may be the way that others _do_ think about ..

> from "if (something) then do (something)" to "if (something) then don't do
> anything, otherwise do (something)". That's a bass-ackwards way of thinking.

Perhaps others think like an EVALUATE or case statement:

   condition1
      action1
   condition2
      ...
   otherwise
      ...

It just happens that this has a condition1 with nothing to do (but may
have later) and there isn't a condition2 (yet, that might come).

IF Input-Date = Prev-Date
        CONTINUE
ELSE
IF Input-Date > Todays-Date
        PERFORM Too-Soon
ELSE
        PERFORM Date-Ritual
END-IF
END-IF

> IF Input-Date NOT EQUAL Prev-Date
>         PERFORM Date-Ritual
> END-IF
>
> is IMO vastly preferable to the construct above.

Others may prefer:

IF NOT ( Input-Date = Prev-Date )
         PERFORM Date-Ritual
END-IF
```

```


<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:f9ad4h$rqp$1@reader2.panix.com...
> In article <1t5hb31h4un0m37etgfn2uri8u98blhb0s@4ax.com>,
> Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>>to.

Exactly. I think most of us have found exactly that in our experience of 
maintaining code. Richard pointed it out several years ago when style wars 
were first started here... :-)

>
> (have a care... next thing you know you may be postulating that people
> tend to do things they see as being in the best interests of themselves
> and those around them... a dark, negative, tedious observation, some say)

It is dark if you don't qualify it.  ("SOME people are motivated by self 
interest") It is certainly negative because many people are NOT just 
motivated by their own best interests (people do selfless things all the 
time and our society would be a great deal different and more unpleasant if 
they didn't; to not acknowledge that is churlish and short-sighted), and to 
make the negative statement without balancing the observation is stating 
ONLY the downside. It is tedious when it is repeated ad nauseum, as an 
entrenched position, admitting no other possibilities.

Now, if you are having a bad day and can only see downsides, let's hope that 
is a temporary thing. If it isn't, then don't be surprised if no-one wants 
to play with you... :-)

>
> I barely know what *I* do, Mr Brazee, let alone 'people'... but I'm
> willing to go out on a limb and say that, more often than not, people are
> accustomed to what they are used to.
>

:-)  True. (and fair...:-))... but hardly profound.

Never mind...it wasn't dark, negative, or tedious...:-)

Pete.
```

```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
news:5hi9caF3jsol6U1@mid.individual.net...
>
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

This is the "School of Over-reaction".  I dare say many of us have been
caught at least once by the NOT .. AND requirement (i.e., IF A NOT = B OR C
should be IF A NOT = B AND C); the over-reaction is ban the NOT because its
use may be counter-intuitive.  (I know there are alternate ways to code this
that make it clear.  That's not the point).

There may in fact be a case to be made for employing only a subset of a
language - the subset necessary for the simplest expression of the problem -
and I don't care to get into specifying that because it's too personal - but
rules declared because of fear tend to stultify the programmer's development
and likely degrade the program as well.

PL
```

```
Well said. I think De Morgans laws have tripped up many programmers at one 
time or another, yet, they are beautiful in their simplicity and elegance if 
one takes the trouble to understand them. I don't want NOT abolished because 
it is part of a "set"; the set that defines a Boolean Algebra.  To hear 
people whio call themselves "programmers" advocating that NOT be abolished, 
because they didn't understand De Morgan. or  because it is 
"counter-intuitive", is like hearing a coal miner suggest that picks should 
not be allowed in the pit because his friend droopped one on his foot and 
shovels are perfectly adequate anyway...
```

```
wonderful rooms and tasty meals' indicates.
```

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:f94fqj$e4u$1@reader2.panix.com...
> In article <5hllucF3l08euU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[62 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Again your lack of imagination astounds me, but I suppose by now, it 
shouldn't...

You see two choices, but there are many.

First of all the code isn't "Utterly Crap Code, code that doesn't do what it 
is supposed to do and puts bad data into Inventory files, bad numbers into 
Accounting files and will cause the company to lose time and money to 
correct all this badness, code that will, under any circumstances, take more 
time to fix than there  is before the milestone arrives" and so the 
programmer could NOT show irrefutable evidence that it was.

If we are arriving at a milestone, the project has been running for some 
time and anyone writing code like you describe would no longer be with us, 
and any damage done by them would have been repaired. I take my 
responsibilities seriously and the buck stops with me. Because of this, I 
have procedures in place to make sure there are no surprises arriving out of 
left field. One of them is to occasionally browse our source libraries and 
check the quality of code for myself. The rubbish you describe would have 
been picked up by myself or one of the team weeks ago.

Second of all, even if it WAS so bad that there wasn't time to fix it before 
the milestone, it would STILL be fixed.

I'd do it myself (and have done... on one memorable occasion when a loyal 
Egyptian named Moustapha ElSawy worked day and night with me over a weekend 
and  we corrected and rewrote an entire system by Monday, and on another 
occasion when two "volunteers" and I did three months work in 3 days (over a 
long weekend); I wrote an access method  (we called it RAM) and the other 
two used it to cut through dozens of programs and have everything working by 
Tuesday... for motivated people there is always an option, and "failure" is 
not it.

However, just for the sake of this excrutiatingly enjoyable argument, let's 
accept your extremely unlikely scenario and say "it can't be done"... So 
what to do?

Implement Utterly Crap code? Not an option.

Slip the milestone? Not an option.

Examine options:

1. How critical is this system? Can we outsource this service so that the 
Clients still get a service, while we do something about the Utterly Crap 
code?

2. If our software can't provide this service can we get a package that can? 
Is there time to implement it?

3. If we apply all available resources (including myself) and spread these 
programs around our team, can we at least get them processing core 
functionality? Crap code is better than Utterly Crap code, until such time 
as we can kick the Crap out of it. (Buys us time and still provides a 
service for our clients, at the time we promised. It may not be as much as 
we promised, immediately, but it will be useful and the full Monty will be 
there very quickly afterwards).

Option 3 seems like a reasonable option so we'll go for that. We'll decide 
what specifically we intend to achieve in the time available and everyone 
will be allocated programs to apply the agreed fixes to. I will contact the 
clients and point out that we have found some problems which we are working 
on and  it means a limited subset of functions will be available on 
milestone day.  The user and I will establish what their priorites are and 
whatever he calls is what will be done first.  The core functions will work 
and will work correctly, we will work out how to work around other scenarios 
that might occur, using a combination of manual and computerized 
procedures., on a temporary basis until we have things repaired.

Having managed his expectations, it is now essential to see they are met, 
and, if possible, exceeded.

If we don't have the resources to get the critical stuff working, I'll bring 
in contractors for the period we have until the milestone. (if it's less 
than a week, our team will be working overtime... all leave is cancelled :-) 
(Later, they get to go somewhere sunny, on the company)

The client manager will work with us, and six months later, when everything 
is fixed and the new system is implemented, what they will remember is that 
we all  (Business and IT) worked hard on their system to get it through a 
bad patch, and I shall make sure that he and his department receive the 
credit they deserve for doing that.. And there will be a party :-)

But the whole scenario is bullshit, because I wouldn't be in that situation 
in the first place.

Do the job or stop taking their money? Yes, I guess you could call it that. 
Certainly I don't take their money unless I can do something for them, and I 
have offered a money-back guarantee on at least one occasion. (No one has 
ever claimed it)

Like I said, I'm not looking for work, and people actually seem to enjoy 
working with me.

So, that's what THIS manager would do. (I notice you spoke for all 
programmers, you are not qualified to do that; I know many programmers who 
are nothing like you, just as you have had many managers who are nothing 
like me.)

And I really don't care whether you protest that I didn't accept your 
scenario and the milestone wasn't met or any other Dwarfish peeve you care 
to come up with... My client is happy, my team are happy, and that makes me 
happy. However, I expect that's a sensation that may be foreign to you.

Pete.
```

```
>
```

```
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
```

```
>>
>> Milestones are not to be missed except in the most extreme circumstances,
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> something up front about which they know little?

I make it a policy to be open and frank. I don't keep secrets from them 
unless I have been told stuff in confidence, and then I tell them I can't 
comment and why I can't. (I keep confidence for anyone who needs it; from 
the Janitor to the CEO, if what you tell me is confidential, it stays that 
way until you say it isn't any more)  I find the input of the team very 
useful and usually take their suggestions regarding planning and 
dependencies.

I expect them to agree or disagree about areas of the plan that fall in 
their domain. (however they are privy to the whole plan so they can see 
dependencies and bottlenecks, and keep things in perspective.)

For example, if the plan requires duration for programming activity, I want 
their input. How can you expect people todeliver something if you just 
arbitrarily tell them it will be done by a certain time, without being 
prepared to discuss what is involved, possible approaches, ways it could be 
shortcut...etc? How can you expect people to buy in to the success of your 
project if they have never seen what you are planning or had a chance to 
discuss or contribute to it?

My role as a manager is to guide, support,  facilitate, and manage. It is 
not to simply dictate and micro-manage every activity, just to stamp my 
Authority on it.

>
>>
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>

That is the single main reason I have moved away from the Waterfall approach 
and into RAD. Waterfall delivers what was agreed, 9 months after it was 
agreed, when it is no longer relevant, but it was agreed. RAD delivers what 
you need right now, including the changes you requested yesterday, without 
problem.
```

```
Yeah, it seems to call for intelligent users and a computer solution
to a computer problem. The problem was users who did not care about
putting in pricing information that was wrong and did not check the
quality of their work. If a user wants to put the price of a loaf of
bread up to $75 then no computer system could stop them. The users'
management claimed to have intelligent users (when we developed a
quick and dirty Godfather Payments reporting system the management
said to not put in any validation on the parameter file because they
had intelligent users who would not make mistakes. We put in some
rudimentary validation and the job fell over on it's first run) and
did not accept that they made mistakes. The reasons for the repeated
failures were:

1.  The introduction of system managed storage which deprived the
system of a dedicated sort pack on nights when a large insurance
company print run grabbed all SYSDA;

2.  The intermediate level of client management, being aware of the
'don't care' attitude of their employees, requested that error
messages be produced and stored with the system abending after x,000
errors had been recorded. They reviewed the errors and then decided to
continue or fix and rerun the suite. It gave me great pleasure to ring
the client manager at home with a cheery 'It's your early morning
call...'. Despite repeatedly waking him up (and his wife) over the
years the errors still continued. The client had asked for a quote for
a report to be developed which could vet the price changes but it
would have been too expensive to do; he certainly never raised a work
request to authorize spending time on thinking the problem through (it
being cheaper to use his 2 days per week of support time up on
callouts than to pay for additional work). <SOUND OF HEAVENLY TRUMPETS
BLOWING> I suspect that, if the client had approved a budget to
develop a solution, I would have been the only company employee
capable of specifying and coding the solution </SOUND OF HEAVENLY
TRUMPETS BLOWING>.

3.  We did, however, develop a price change security system which
prevented prices from being altered under certain conditions (like for
major customers), but that didn't stop the errors on other customers'
prices.

> maintenance under pressure, is the leading category.

Don't confuse CALLOUT and MAINTENANCE. Maintenance does introduce
errors and they may occur at errors-per-line rates far higher than
development rates but, in my experience, callout error rates (a subset
of maintenance error rates) are much lower.

>
> > 3.  I have worked with many people, of various skill levels, and with
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> of overnight fixes"?

Because the development teams produced faulty solutions. You may
recall when I twittered on about a developer who put an untested
system into live because he had run out of budget for testing. It is
also an axiomatic truth that developers are not interested in finding
bugs, rather they are interested in proving that their solution is
correct. Therefore, they do not test thoroughily and skimping allows
errors to creep through. Further, isn't it true that there is no such
thing as a bug-free program, rather that any program said to be bug-
free just hasn't fallen over yet?

>

…[6 more quoted lines elided]…

Quite possibly. The error rates went down after developers took
responsibility for post-implementation fixes. Their managers also saw
the results of their employees' shoddy work, previously hidden by the
fact that ops did not report development errors back to the
developers.

My clients would have had a fit if you said that to them. An 8 hour
delay became a 24 hour delay in delivery of the invoice to the client.
If the average debt-age is 30 days then you have just increased the
debt-age by 3.333 percent which would work out as a significant amount
of interest.

questions I should ask are how high and in which direction? Sorry, I

as his justification for agreeing impossible deadlines for
developments which put the team under repeated and unnecessary levels
of stress.
```

```
You should have seen some of my payroll and marketing programs written
to fit in 16K to 24K partitions in the late 1960's and early 1970's.
PERFORM was simulated by using ALTER SWnnnn-FUNCTION TO
nnnn-FOLLOWING-PARAGRAPH.  There were no PERFORMs.  This was to save
memory when using the DOS360 compiler.  Unfortunately some of the
marketing programs survived until at least the early 1990's.  They
were efficient in terms of memory utilization but a horror from a
maintenance point of view.  I did document all settings of each switch
which was identified with the letters SW but I hope that I would find
a better way of achieving the same goal.
```

```
On Thu, 2 Aug 2007 13:48:46 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>
…[99 more quoted lines elided]…
>I never saw it done on a 360. BALR on 14 is hardly a big overhead :-)

Unfortunately, the BALR didn't work like either the IBM 7080 Recieve
Serial followed by a Transfer and store location or the RCA 301
Transfer to X after storing return in Y.  Thus a 7080 or an RCA 301
could branch to the beginning of a Perform range setting the exit for
the return to next instruction with the exit being able to reset
itself.  Since branch and link stored the return address in a
register, this could not be done so the code for PERFORM was 

MVC  HOLDADR,EXITINS+2     save the return value in the current exit
instruction
MVC  EXITINS+2(2),RETRNADR  set the exit instruction to branch to the
return point
B    PERFPARA              branch to the performed paragraph
MVC  EXITINS+2(2),HOLDADR  restore the exit instruction.  

For those into the arcanity of 360 assembler RETRNADR was an S-con in
register displacement format.  The COBOL edited move also seemed to
generate excessive code. 

>
>Unfortunately some of the
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pete.
```

```
The only thing I trust when it comes to program code (in any language) is 
what I see in the interactive debugger, as it runs...

Pete.
```

```
Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1186019993.417899.323910@x35g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
> On Aug 2, 1:19 pm, "tlmfru" <la...@mts.net> wrote:
> > Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>> > > <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
No I didn't!!! This has been badly snipped and attributes statements to me 
which were actually made by Richard.

>> > > Yes, it certainly will be within the source, but that is a point I
>> > > have made before.  If, for example, GO TOs are allowed from any point
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Peter, a quick note on sarcasm, not to be taken personally, and not meant to 
wound....

I have heard sarcasm described as "the lowest form of wit" and a particular 
English tutor was fond of repeating: "Sarcasm is a weakness." Now you're 
saying "it ill becomes us..."

Poor old sarcasm. It is definitely the poor relation when it comes to 
rhetoric. And yet, it seems to me that the people who decry it, generally 
are not capable of using it.

I find things amusing. I find them amusing whether they are based on 
sarcasm, whether they are "frowned upon" or not, whether they are in good 
taste or not, whether they are politically correct or not.

To me, funny is funny.

Some of the funniest things we all love to smile at are based on sarcasm. I 
don't think anyone would have said Basil Fawlty's sarcasm ill became him: 
"You should go on Mastermind, Dear. Sybil Fawlty, specialist subject 
'stating the bleeding obvious'..." or the famous speech about the view from 
the hotel room in Torquay with references to "herds of thundering 
Wildebeest" and so on. It makes us laugh.

Personally, I don't think Richard's response was so much sarcastic, as 
simply "dry wit", but I agree it is a fine line.

Of course, on the relatively rare occasions when I resort to sarcasm, (I can 
get by on wit most of the time) it is just "irony". Only when others do it 
is it sarcasm...:-)

If it makes us laugh, or even smile, then I'm for it, and if people use 
sarcasm against me, I have a pretty full box of tricks to deal with that.

As Sir Arthur Wing Pinero remarked in the last line of his play "Dandy 
Dick": "For there is no harm in laughter."

Pete.
```

```
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> >> > > Yes, it certainly will be within the source, but that is a point I

point

> >> > > program works is by examining the whole program looking for Go TOs

> > compiler listings include (perhaps only on demand) a listing of
paragraphs
> > with a cross-reference of where they're referenced - and by what verb.
> > And
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Peter, a quick note on sarcasm, not to be taken personally, and not meant
to
> wound....

Not taken personally.  But I think you missed the point of my post.  I meant
to signal that >>I<< was going to be sarcastic in what I said.

> Some of the funniest things we all love to smile at are based on sarcasm.
I
> don't think anyone would have said Basil Fawlty's sarcasm ill became him:
> "You should go on Mastermind, Dear. Sybil Fawlty, specialist subject
> 'stating the bleeding obvious'..." or the famous speech about the view
from
> the hotel room in Torquay with references to "herds of thundering
> Wildebeest" and so on. It makes us laugh.

John Cleese at his best produces matchless humour - and so far as Basil
Fawlty  is concerned he (John) is on record as asking how can we feel sorry
for this truly appalling man?  Basil hates everybody and makes no secret of
it; the way he expresses that hate is , I think, is what we find so funny.

>
> Personally, I don't think Richard's response was so much sarcastic, as
> simply "dry wit", but I agree it is a fine line.
>

Well, I didn't.  I took it as sneering, which was unnecessary.  There were
lots of other things he could have said to take the piss out of me without
causing offence.

Humour is a dicey thing at best.  Even the gentlest, mildest comic strips,
for instance (such as "Rose is Rose") make fun of something - even if it's
only a nice little boy's lack of knowledge of the world.  Humour invariably
MAKES FUN of something or someone.  (With the possible exception of puns.  I
haven't decided yet why they are so funny).  So naturally its acceptability
is never a given.

> If it makes us laugh, or even smile, then I'm for it,

So am I, of course, but I have to use my own definition.  And while I gave
up zingers for New Years a long time ago, I reserve the right to be at least
a little nasty if I feel it's called for.


> Pete.

PL
```

```
the tv show 7th Heaven.  Well, not quite that bad.  I guess I just prefer my
comics strips to be funny.

Frank
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ALTER design (Was: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through)

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

- **Message-ID:** `<f8vscp$22v$1@reader2.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <ERmsi.148737$wG2.108969@newsfe17.lga> <5hfn3qF3kjtfmU1@mid.individual.net> <D5Ksi.30444$GO6.4640@newsfe21.lga>`

```
In article <D5Ksi.30444$GO6.4640@newsfe21.lga>, tlmfru <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

[snip]

>Humour invariably
>MAKES FUN of something or someone.

I'm not quite sure what to make of this, Mr Lacey... are you positing a 
milder variant of 'all humor is displaced sadism'?  Consider the a 
statement such as:

'I've studied this 'humor' stuff very, very seriously.'

... and see if it raises a smile, given the lack of congruence between 
'humor' and 'very, very serious study'.  Next, of course, comes:

'I've studied this 'humor' stuf very, very seriously... and I don't find 
anything funny about it at all!'

... and see if this raises a slightly greater smile... and a sense of 
pity.  What is sad about not being able to get a joke?

(it might be that 'sad' is equally as rational a response as 'funny'... 
and since both are emotional states one might be disappointed by analysing 
them rationally)

DD
```

```
On Fri, 3 Aug 2007 13:01:37 -0500, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

> Humour invariably
>MAKES FUN of something or someone. 

"MAKES FUN" is often used as a term of meanness - but I prefer to take
the words literally in your context.

The best humor are the jokes that show us life - that we can empathize
with.   The joke is on us.    We very much enjoy the tribulations of
our children and grandchildren learning about life and responding like
children - without making fun of them.

When a humorous work has someone going through the troubles or
embarrassment that we go through, the humor is especially fun.

It can also work when it shows someone having our fantasies coming
true.

>(With the possible exception of puns.  I
>haven't decided yet why they are so funny).

Aha moments can be funny - seeing a different relationship than we
expect.   The story about the boy who finds a dozen ways to measure
the height of a building using a barometer is funny.   Again, it is a
learning situation for us.
```

```
In an analysis of my reactions I see that laughter is usually caused
by creating a conflift in the brain and then resolving it. Puns are
usually only funny the first time they are used because they don't
subsequently create any conflict.

I find there is a general difference between american and british
comedy. Obviously there is a great deal of cross over, but typically a
good 'british' comedy will laugh at themselves while american comedy
may laugh at someone else.
```

```
On 4 Aug, 00:35, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
> In an analysis of my reactions I see that laughter is usually caused
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I may be out of date but my understanding of laughter is that it is a
nervous reaction induced by relief at NOT being the butt of the joke.
The rictus (or grin) produced in a laugh is similar to the rictus
produced by dogs and chimps when admitting subordination to a superior
in a conflict situation.

There is a difference in styles of comedy (US v UK). It is often noted
(on the east bank of the pond) that the UK comics can laugh at
themselves but that US comics don't. Also, whereas we are happy to
take the mickey out of the Queen (gawd bless 'er maj), the
establishment and almost anything else we can think of, the US comics
appear to be a little unwilling to lay into Bush et al.

Finally, I don't approve of UK comedies being redone in the US for
your domestic audience. It seems to me to lose something in the
translation (the Office is a classic example - it just is not funny
with American actors).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ALTER design (Was: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through)

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2007-08-01T13:28:33+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5ha5u2F3i8a3jU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <1185663930.742550.293780@z24g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <13ao1utdpul1cc@corp.supernews.com> <5h3djvF3imk7tU1@mid.individual.net> <3i3sa3hcmfal9nepkknnke9cq2eu4981c5@4ax.com> <5h78qqF3hguocU1@mid.individual.net> <4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com>`

```
"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:4MWdnUDRHLIlPTPbnZ2dnUVZ_h2pnZ2d@comcast.com...
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Cheeky monkey :-)!

> My first experience with the ALTER verb was our shop standards when I
> first got there.  "The use of ALTER is strictly forbidden."  :)  I
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Now, see, that's a really good question. So much more interesting than
simply arguing about the relative merits of ALTER.:-)

I'll attempt to give you an answer that excludes the things already covered,
like first time thru switches, although these are worth looking at for a
moment, in passing...

If you put an IF statement at the start of  a piece of code (maybe even a
SECTION... :-)) that says:

IF FIRST-TIME-THRU
     SET NOT-FIRST-TIME-THRU TO TRUE
     PERFORM TAKE-THE-LID-OFF
END-IF
PERFORM POUR-A-MEASURE
...

And this section gets executed  (by PERFORM or GO TO) 10 million times, you
are doing a compare that can never be true 9,999,999 times. Nowadays, that
isn't such a big deal, but there was a time when it was...

If, instead, you were to formulate it as:

FIRST-TIME.
      GO TO REMOVE-LID.
REMOVE-LID.
       ALTER FIRST-TIME TO PROCEED TO OTHER-TIMES
       PERFORM TAKE-THE-LID-OFF.
OTHER-TIMES.
       PERFORM POUR-A-MEASURE
...

You have removed 9,999,999 instructions from the execution. Now it just does
a branch instead of a compare. On most platforms this is the quickest
instruction in the set. (Almost invariably, a single machine instruction).
Compares on some machines can be horrific, generating up to a dozen machine
instructions (especially if data conversion is required before the compare
can take place.)

Can you honestly say that the second example above is much more complex than
the first? Or so "horrendous" it couldn't be maintained by a trained COBOL
Programmer? :-)

It doesn't even take much more writing, yet it saves 9,999,999 compare
instructions, and is maybe 12 times more efficient than your IF switch....

Some people would call that "good" :-)

YOUR QUESTION:

> Now my curiosity is more theoretical.  How does one think to design ALTER
> in a good way?

It isn't really about designing ALTER, it is about approaching things in a
different way.

I'll rephrase your question thus:

"So, what kind of mindset do you need to deal with COBOL that
modifies itself as it runs?"

MY ANSWER:

Something close to the old Assembler mindset where instructions in memory
were frequently modified (not just the Branch instructions) to save time and
space.

You need a non-linear mindset that is imaginative and intelligent. What
DeBono calls "Lateral" thinking,  but not only must you think "outside the
box" you must do it "on the hop".   Some people really enjoy this way of
working and are very good at it, most do not and are not... It is definitely
not for the faint-hearted...(That's what I meant when I said "the wimps won
the day") :-)

Now let's look at a few cases where the ALTER solution is probably the most
elegant one available...

(Most of these CAN be implemented without using ALTER, but, in my opinion, 
implementing them WITH ALTER actually added significant value.)

Consider a dynamic process where a series of  "stages" are executed in a
variable sequence, but only as the execution proceeds, can the subsequent
stage requirements be decided. Normally, we would write a program that
controlled the flow, tracked each item through each stage,  and could cater
for every possible "path". That would be linear.

But what about when the "path" is actually a network and the possibilities
are approaching infinity? We have little choice but to cause the stages to
be switched as we proceed through them. "Fast switching"...rings a bell...
sure there used to be something in COBOL that could do that...Oops! Not any
more... :-)

What about a state transition processor?  You have a bunch of, say, 700 odd
keywords for COBOL and you need to parse a COBOL source program. You COULD
lookup every word the parser encountered against the Reserved word list.
That would be linear. (It would also be pretty slow).

Suppose instead you had a series of transition diagrams that showed the 
states you are interested in. Suppose further you had a series of "State 
Evaluation" engines  (Modules that decide what the current letter of the 
word is and how this relates from context, to something we may or may not be 
interested in pursuing. These modules need to dynamically switch the 
sequence of processing, depending on what the current  state within the 
current context is. It's a bit too complex to go into detail here (I wrote 
an article about it many years ago, but it is on a different computer in an 
attic somewhere.) )

Anyway, perhaps this very general description is enough for you to get the 
idea. (I hasten to add that such processes do NOT get maintained in source 
code (apart from maybe adding new state/context process sequences when new 
Reserved words are added to the language)  The solution described is still 
linear, but it is dynamically linear and, again, requires fast switching 
within a given context.
(This particular one can be implemented without ALTER by a slightly modified 
approach, but ALTER does it faster.)

Consider a series of "parameters" that arrive independently (or not) from 
different sources,and conflict with each other. (This is non-linear)You 
start the process then realise it has just been made redundant by a new 
conflicting parameter set. So, it needs to be dynamically 
modified...Otherwise, what has been done so far is wasted. You'd wait until 
all the paremeter sets have arrived before starting? What if you can't do 
that? Suppose there is no way of  knowing if or when these things may 
arrive? Suppose the process is driving machines that cannot be switched off 
without requiring expensive restarts? It's a dynamic process that needs a 
dynamic solution. ALTER is a very good way to dynamically modify a process 
chain, even if it is already under way.

What about a railway network with  various sets of points? (I find this a 
very helpful way to visualize systems that have ALTERs in them).  In order 
to get to a specific destination you need to change point settings. You 
could do this before you start (if you know what the destination is and it 
won't change when you are on the journey), or, you might need to keep your 
options open and accept any one of several destinations, depending on what 
happens en route.

If you go to Europe and are in say, England, you can book train  travel to 
almost any European destination from almost any station in the U.K. 
Alternate routings are provided instantly and the system talks to mainframes 
and client/server networks all over Europe. This is achieved by a ticketing 
system which was developed to coincide with the opening of the Channel 
Tunnel. It is called TRIBUTE and I worked on it. The English part of it is 
written in MicroFocus COBOL and uses dynamic switching to book each leg of a 
journey. A journey can be "defined" simply by giving values for each of the 
"switch points" in the network. (in effect, ALTERing them as the journey 
progresses) . Each node will then do the ticketing for that country/region 
It is totally flexible and dynamic.

If you think about a process that is like the described railway network and 
has, say, 10 "switching points", each of which is two-states (in reality, 
processes can switch to many more than 2 destinations, but this will do just 
to stimulate thought), then there are 2 **10 possible ways through the 
process. Would you code 2 ** 10 IF statements...? I don't think so. Each 
item to be processed is "defined" with a "pack" of 10 ALTER statements. 
These are executed and then the process is run. (You might think of mice 
running through an adjustable maze that has 10 doors in it; you set the 
doors how you want them, then release the mouse :-))

So these are just some of the cases where ability to modify a process 
dynamically can be considered "useful".

I'm not suggesting we should bring back ALTER (although I wouldn't mind if 
it was re-instated), I think I am just mourning the fact that programming 
gets "dumbed down" to the lowest common denominator because some people are 
not capable of handling power tools.
(These are usually the same ones who bleat about "ease of maintenance", 
then never document what they write or format it so it is readable...)

(I'm sure most people reading this have worked on sites with restrictive 
programming standards that were brought in because somebody couldn't follow 
something, or somebody thought that "complex" constructs might be 
dangerous...It is the same syndrome; if a dummy can do it we are all that 
much safer... Perish the thought that people might be taught or trained how 
to safely use power tools...)

Even if I never used it, I'd like to think the tool was available, in case I 
DID need it.

Solutions are often proscribed by the tools we use (Whether they SHOULD be 
or not is a subject for a different discussion...)

I've just accepted a job that involves writing some more powerful tools for 
a Database conversion.  Without these tools it would be two years work. With 
the tools, hopefully, not more than 6 months. Time to write the tools? 
Around one month. (I'll assist with using them as well... so I'm committed 
to this for more than a month :-)) The point is that tools empower us and 
enable us to reduce time, money, and effort. (I can't imagine how I got by 
without VS 2005; this tool has increased my productivity at least threefold, 
but it is hard to quantify or prove.) Removing tools from the box is always 
sad in my book. Doing it because of a "Nanny" mentality just sucks...

OK, Daniel, I hope you might review your feelings about ALTER (a construct 
you have probably never been allowed to use :-)). As you can see there is at 
least one person (actually, I'm reminded that Howard never had a problem 
with it either so I'm sure there are more, but I can't speak for them :-)) 
who never got unwrapped about ALTER, or spaghetti programming, or SECTIONs, 
or GO TOs, but simply saw it all as part of the "job". Different styles, 
some "better", some "worse", but realizing that these words are totally 
subjective.

Got some convoluted  code you can't figure out? Give it to the 
professionals.

It's only COBOL... :-)

Pete.
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message

> wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Different types of arcana.

No, my experience is that there is less arcana, and more explanation and 
example.

Given the true meaning of "arcana", there is much in COBOL that is hidden. 
It isn't "secret" it just isn't available openly, because the user base (in 
Internet terms) is "small". It isn't worth writing articles about it and 
explaining it because the ROI on doing so is minimal.

Also, much of what is pertinent to COBOL, simply isn't pertinent to the 
world outside of COBOL. Furthermore, as we have seen here recently, there is 
fierce division over COBOL styles and so there will be argument even if you 
DID try and explain some of the conceptual stuff in simple, easily 
assimilable language. (It is a couple of years now since I wrote anything 
publicly on COBOL; it just isn't worth the effort...)

Let me try and explain with an example from what I am actually doing at the 
moment.

I couldn't understand why, having created a cookie and set the expiry date, 
if I later tested that expiry, it was inexplicably reset to the mininum 
DateTime. I traced it in VS 2005 and could see my compare failing every 
time, and the Expires property of the cookie showing the minimum date. (even 
though it showed the correct expiry date when I created it...)

Instead of consulting ASP.NET help I simply GOOGLEd... [C# ASP.NET cookie 
expiry] Immediately, an article describing in detail exactly what I was 
trying to do, comprehensive C# example code, and in 20 minutes I was back on 
the road, with my cookies now working correctly. (The problem was in my 
understanding of the word "Expires" and how I expected it to behave...)

With this kind of "Help" at your fingertips, you don't need to visit forums 
for support very often, and when you do it is usually because you are 
pioneering something, or want to compare notes with others who've done what 
you did, or need more general background, or something simply isn't working 
the way it is supposed to. Many MicroSoft people frequent these forums and 
they are happy to help. Some of the information is remarkable. They also 
post on what is going on and offer sample code. I received assistance from 
one such person for what was not even a MS problem (it was a problem with 
the Fujitsu NetCOBOL Run Time optimization). He didn't solve my problem for 
me, but he shed insight into it and pointed me in the right direction so I 
could solve it. And I did, and learned much in the process... :-)

So the nature of the forums is different from what we have here. The average 
age of the people who frequent the forums is lower than we have here too, 
and that means a different mindset... :-)

Pete.
```

```
I think they are comprised of younger people who are much more concerned 
with getting solutions than arguing about style, or reminiscing on the 
"Glory Days" (they aren't old enough to have had any, for the most part 
:-)), or arguing for the sake of rhetoric (many can't spell or speak English 
very well and it isn't their first language, so they use it only for the 
exchange of information, not for fun...), and the nature of the programming 
is such that there really isn't that much variation in style anyway. Because 
code is more succinct and less verbose, you can look at it and see 
immediately whether it is useful or not. That is all that seems to really 
matter.

There is a different atmosphere in the C# forums. I made no secret of being 
a "COBOL guy" and only encountered one hostile reaction which was quickly 
jumped on by others. For the most part, I was made welcome and received 
help. I look forward to being able to reciprocate when I have acquired 
enough expertise. I already have been able to help in some very specific 
areas like COM Interop with C#, but I can see that my level of expertise is 
growing. I look at code I wrote 3 months ago and quickly change it to be 
more efficient. The full richness of the component paradigm is starting to 
really sink in and I am very happy and comfortable in the C# space.

Having said that, it is more fun to post here... :-) (Well, most of the 
time...:-))
```

```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:eg2sa3dfhomcm0nv9jd37o4h7ghq6eet45@4ax.com...
> On Fri, 27 Jul 2007 23:42:30 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> decades old "structured programming",
[Snipped reference to EXIT PARAGRAPH, which is
irrelevant to the discussion.]

Mr Brazee, if your compiler does not have EXIT SECTION,
it may be simulated on any conforming COBOL 85 compiler.

What was described is entirely consistent with "structured
programming". Each section has a single-entry and single-exit
and each paragraph within the section has a single-entry and
single-exit.

Given that a "mini-program" may have a well-defined interface
and its functionality may be implemented either as a section or
as a paragraph group, I submit that the difference may be
characterized graphically as this: When implemented as a section,
the "mini-program" is presented as a box made of a solid line,
and, when implemented as a paragraph group, a box made of a
dashed line.

I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
will treat each section as an entity. I chose "dashed line" to
represent paragraph groups because the compiler will ignore
the comments that may delineate the groups.

Without the well-defined interface, one might as well surround
everything with dotted lines.

The choices are these: solid, dashed, or dotted!
```

```
On Mon, 30 Jul 2007 14:14:03 -0400, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
wrote:

>Mr Brazee, if your compiler does not have EXIT SECTION,
>it may be simulated on any conforming COBOL 85 compiler.

My goals here are:

1.  Do not have any drop through from one paragraph to the next.
2.  Don't have a bunch of switches to emulate the exit statement.

>I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
>will treat each section as an entity. I chose "dashed line" to
>represent paragraph groups because the compiler will ignore
>the comments that may delineate the groups.

My worry isn't the compiler, which does whatever we tell it to do - it
is the maintenance programmer, who might not notice that the paragraph
he ads can be inadvertently reached by the section above it.

To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
through process is counter to structured programming and easy
maintenance.
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:fefsa3tko050t39dj8t5r8e2olq6c2oalp@4ax.com...

> 2.  Don't have a bunch of switches to emulate the exit statement.

Neither of these are violated, except as noted below.

> >I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
> >will treat each section as an entity. I chose "dashed line" to
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> he ads can be inadvertently reached by the section above it.

I don't see this as realistic. When a program is built of
sections. Code changes are made to sections or sections
are added. If a programmer doesn't notice that, well ....

In any case, where a section includes performed paragraphs,
adding a paragraph means placing that paragraph within
the same section. This is no different than making certain
that a paragraph added to a paragraphs-only program is
placed in the same program!

> To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
> through process is counter to structured programming and easy
> maintenance.

There is no "drop through" except in certain special cases
with GO TO DEPENDING, which could also happen with
a paragraphs-only program; because the "drop through" is
intentional.
```

```
In article <13asqtvhafjldf5@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:

>I don't see this as realistic. When a program is built of
>sections. Code changes are made to sections or sections
>are added. If a programmer doesn't notice that, well ....

Isn't that part of the problem, though? One *more* thing for the maintenance 
programmer to have to notice. Numerous studies have shown that somewhere 
around 90% of the life-cycle cost of most software is in maintenance. This 
means that when writing software, ease of maintenance should be the paramount 
stylistic concern, head and shoulders above every other consideration. 
(Real-time and embedded systems are obvious exceptions, but of course those 
would be off-topic in a Cobol group anyway. <g>)
>
>In any case, where a section includes performed paragraphs,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>placed in the same program!

Sorry, but that's simply not so. When adding a paragraph to a paragraphs-only 
program, it's impossible to *not* place it in the same program. It certainly 
*is* possible to place a paragraph in the wrong SECTION, though. It's also 
possible to place a paragraph in the wrong *position* in the *right* SECTION.
>
>> To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>intentional.

So because it's intentional, it doesn't exist? Drop-through is a bad idea, 
even if it *is* intentional.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

```

"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message
news:IeFri.46485$YL5.42322@newssvr29.news.prodigy.net...
> In article <13asqtvhafjldf5@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith"
<ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
>
> >I don't see this as realistic. When a program is built of
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Isn't that part of the problem, though? One *more* thing for the
maintenance
> programmer to have to notice. Numerous studies have shown that somewhere
> around 90% of the life-cycle cost of most software is in maintenance. This
> means that when writing software, ease of maintenance should be the
paramount
> stylistic concern, head and shoulders above every other consideration.

In other words, building the program from paragraphs only
eases maintenance? I think not; but let me explain.

One of the points I have "dancing" around is the relation
between design and implementation. Faithful implementation
of a design would seem to require a style that mirrors the
style of the design. What might one do when implementing
an object-oriented design in COBOL 85, for example?

The elements of OOD include (sub)classes and methods.
Using an OOPL one would make class-names distinct and
methods non-distinct; that is, the same method-name may be
used with any (sub)class. Robert C. Martin, "Designing
Object-Oriented C++ Applications ...", suggests, indirectly,
that OOD is an inversion of procedural design. Why not,
then, invert the OOD when using procedural implementation?
That is, make method-names distinct and class-names
non-distinct.

Following is a outline of the procedural pattern for implementing
OOD in COBOL 85. I use braces to enclose the variable parts.

{method-name} section.
begin.
    evaluate class-type
        when {class-name}-id
            perform {class-name}
        ...
    end-evaluate
    exit section (or its equivalent)

{class-name}.
    {method code}.

Sections are used because class-names are non-distinct and
the compiler will resolve those names correctly. [Other
details are omitted because this is only a presentation of
a concept; not a how-to book.]

In terms of maintenance, adding a new class (which uses an
existing method-name) means making changes to the section,
and, adding a new method means adding a section. Thus
continued ease of maintenance, for this case, means adhering
to the implementation of the object-oriented design. There is,
simply, no room for mixing design methods.

The choice between sections and paragraphs-only depends
on design, continued ease of maintenance depends on the
programmer being both familiar with and faithful to the design.
To answer the question initially presented, building a program
from paragraphs only is not the only reasonable choice and
does not guarantee ease of maintenance.

> (Real-time and embedded systems are obvious exceptions, but of course
those
> would be off-topic in a Cobol group anyway. <g>)

My primary job responsibility, for more than 20 years,
was embedded systems programming. Maintaining COBOL
and C applications was secondary. Sometimes my
experience with embedded systems might spill over into
COBOL. Or, as some may say "The apple doesn't fall far
from the tree".

> >In any case, where a section includes performed paragraphs,
> >adding a paragraph means placing that paragraph within
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Sorry, but that's simply not so. When adding a paragraph to a
paragraphs-only
> program, it's impossible to *not* place it in the same program. It
certainly
> *is* possible to place a paragraph in the wrong SECTION, though. It's also
> possible to place a paragraph in the wrong *position* in the *right*
SECTION.

Novice programmer: "I changed the program like you wanted.
    I added the IF condition and the perform where you
    suggested and added the paragraph to the end of the
    source code, just like I learned. But the compiler can't
    find the paragraph!"
Senior programmer: "Didn't they teach you about nested
    programs? That program is nested! You added the
    paragraph to the wrong program!"

Many impossible things are possible given the correct set
of assumptions! <G>

> >> To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
> >> through process is counter to structured programming and easy
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> even if it *is* intentional.

No! I did not say "it doesn't exist". I said it exists in
"certain special cases ...".

Calling something a "bad idea" without knowing the
circumstances for "special case" seems rather subjective
(though many other words may be used).

The special case involves loop-unrolling to increase
speed. I mentioned it because I was aware of it. If you
like, think of it as something a real-time or embedded
systems programmer might come up with when using
COBOL. <G>

When I used it, the program ran about 80% faster.
I reviewed the program about a year ago and removed
the GO TO DEPENDING choosing instead to store
additional data in each record, and with other changes
the program ran about 10% faster. I then made some
additional changes including unrolling another loop (using
repeating IF/END-IF blocks). The current program
is now running about 150% faster than when I was
using the GO TO DEPENDING.
```

```
> >> >> To me, the whole concept of bundling multiple paragraphs in a drop
> >> >> through process is counter to structured programming and easy
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…

> >> even if it *is* intentional.

> SECTIONs are *inherently* drop-through structures. PERFORMing a SECTION
causes
> each paragraph in it to be executed sequentially, except as modified by GO

> which is another bad idea.

Sections are no more "*inherently* drop-through" than
paragraphs. Performing a section (or paragraph) causes
a transfer of control to the first statement. What happens
after that depends entirely on the statements that are
executed. Specifically, there is no rule regarding sections
that requires each paragraph in a section to be executed
sequentially (The exceptions are irrelevant because the
premise is false.). The rule is that statements be executed
sequentially and that rule applies to paragraphs and
sections, equally.
```

```
In article <13avknk1fk9e1a0@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:

>Sections are no more "*inherently* drop-through" than
>paragraphs. 

That is incorrect.

>Performing a section (or paragraph) causes
>a transfer of control to the first statement. What happens
>after that depends entirely on the statements that are
>executed. 

Performing a paragraph causes a transfer of control to the first statement in 
the paragraph. Absent any further transfers of control, at the end of that 
paragraph, control returns to the statement following the PERFORM.

Performing a section causes a transfer of control to the first statement in 
the first paragraph of the section. Absent any further transfers on control, 
at the end of that paragraph, control drops through to the first statement of 
the subsequent paragraph.

>Specifically, there is no rule regarding sections
>that requires each paragraph in a section to be executed
>sequentially (The exceptions are irrelevant because the
>premise is false.). 

The difference lies in the default behavior. In a section, the default 
behavior is to drop through.

>The rule is that statements be executed
>sequentially and that rule applies to paragraphs and
>sections, equally.

Incorrect, for reasons already described.
>
>
>
```

```
On Aug 1, 4:44 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

> [snip]
>
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> So, according to you the standard is incorrect.

Where the argument is at cross-puroposes is in understanding what is
menat by each in the term 'drop-through'.

Both sections and pargraphs will drop through the lines of code within
them. In the absense of any other control a performed section will
drop-through the paragraph labels within that section (including any
paragraphs after what is intended to be the end of the section).

In the case of the structure you have said you use with:

       PERFORM ABC
       ...

    ABC SECTION.
       PERFORM A
       PERFORM B
       ....
    A.
       ...
    B.
       ...
    SomeOther SECTION.

Then the natural tendancy, in the absense of explicit control, is to
drop through A and B until SomeOther is reached (and through that too
if the SECTION word is missing).

OTOH if the word SECTION is removed from ABC then there will _not_ be
a drop through to A and B and the return will be directly from the end
of ABC _without_ any explicit control statement.

Of course you add an explicit EXIT SECTION change of control to avoid
the drop through (unless you forget it).

You seem to think that 'default' means having an 'EXIT SECTION' in the
first paragraph while what it actually means is 'without explicit
control statements', thus you fail to see the argument being made by
Doug.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
