- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13b0cfmlcf39825@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <eg2sa3dfhomcm0nv9jd37o4h7ghq6eet45@4ax.com> <13asanp7chflce6@corp.supernews.com> <fefsa3tko050t39dj8t5r8e2olq6c2oalp@4ax.com> <13asqtvhafjldf5@corp.supernews.com> <IeFri.46485$YL5.42322@newssvr29.news.prodigy.net> <13autmji2v05a09@corp.supernews.com> <%JLri.1722$ox5.147@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com> <13avknk1fk9e1a0@corp.supernews.com> <JtRri.479$IE5.467@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <13b043eg9h0ql4d@corp.supernews.com> <1185945332.451259.320410@m37g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1185945332.451259.320410@m37g2000prh.googlegroups.com...
[snip]
> You seem to think that 'default' means having an 'EXIT SECTION' in the
> first paragraph while what it actually means is 'without explicit
> control statements', thus you fail to see the argument being made by
> Doug.

No, I know that "default", in the context, means "failure
to act". While one may override the default behavior by
a PERFORM statement or avoid the default behavior by
explicit control statements; that does not, in any way,
change the default behavior. Thus, Mr Miller's statement,
"In a section, the default behavior is to drop through." is
incorrect. "[T]o drop through" is the default behavior of
paragraphs wherever they occur in a COBOL program.

I suppose what Mr Miller should have said is that
performing single paragraphs only guarantees that no
paragraph will exhibit its default behavior by dropping
through to the following paragraph. This is a strong and
sensible reason for using paragraphs only. Instead, he
took a negative approach by trying to attack sections
and those who use them ("[they] should be shot").
Unfortunately, his arguments were so full of errors, both
of commission and of omission, that I find it impossible
to see his argument against sections.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net>
- **Date:** 2007-07-31T19:39:34-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jKSdndgp06PBeTLbnZ2dnUVZ_qWtnZ2d@comcast.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<%JLri.1722$ox5.147@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <eg2sa3dfhomcm0nv9jd37o4h7ghq6eet45@4ax.com> <13asanp7chflce6@corp.supernews.com> <fefsa3tko050t39dj8t5r8e2olq6c2oalp@4ax.com> <13asqtvhafjldf5@corp.supernews.com> <IeFri.46485$YL5.42322@newssvr29.news.prodigy.net> <13autmji2v05a09@corp.supernews.com> <%JLri.1722$ox5.147@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`

```
Doug Miller wrote:
> 
> I suspected something of the sort. Cobol's paragraph structure affords a 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> And that does *not* make for good Cobol.

[snip]

>> The special case involves loop-unrolling to increase
>> speed. I mentioned it because I was aware of it. If you
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Exactly so. And that's the problem. :-)

Who is the arbiter of "good" COBOL, or whether a coding style is a 
"problem" or not?  If someone's code does what it is supposed to do, and 
they document it well, I really don't see the problem.

Take these two loops...  (FETCH is turned into a database call by a 
preprocessor, and returns its results in an alphanumeric variable called 
ERROR-NUM - "0013" means "end of chain".  If you're at the front of the 
set, feching NEXT or FIRST will give you the same record.)

[inline]
*>  Process all equipment for this unit
     FETCH FIRST EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET

     PERFORM UNTIL ERROR-NUM NOT = ZEROES
          PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP
          FETCH NEXT EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET
     END-PERFORM

     IF ERROR-NUM NOT = "0013"
         GO TO ERROR-HANDLER
     END-IF.

That's the way I would write it in COBOL 85, a priming read, and one 
error check.  (Well, not in all-caps, but the logic is similar.)  Now, 
here's a common way we find them in our COBOL 74 code...

100-PROCESS-EQPP.
     FETCH NEXT EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET.

     IF ERROR-NUM = "0013"
         GO TO 100-PROCESS-EQPP-EXIT
     ELSE
         IF ERROR-NUM NOT = ZEROES
             GO TO ERROR-HANDLER.

     PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP.

     GO TO 100-PROCESS-EQPP.

100-PROCESS-EQPP-EXIT.
     EXIT.

Now, I don't particularly care for this style - but is it bad COBOL?  I 
don't know - it requires a PERFORM THRU, but it's pretty small - to mess 
this up you'd pretty much have to try to do it.  It could be written 
differently...

100-PROCESS-EQPP.
     FETCH FIRST EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET.

     PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP
       UNTIL ERROR-NUM > ZEROES.

     IF ERROR-NUM NOT = "0013"
         GO TO ERROR-HANDLER.

This is certainly smaller, and looks more like the COBOL 85 version, but 
I don't like it.  We've now made PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP be responsible for 
getting the "next" equipment record - we've split the loop.  This 
program may even have two options - one for a specific piece of 
equipment, and another that walks all equipment for a unit.

I would postulate that the middle loop, given the constraints of COBOL 
74, works fine; although, as there are two tests of ERROR-NUM for each 
iteration, it may not be the most efficient.  And I would also postulate 
that the same code would compile and work fine on a 2002-compliant 
compiler as well.

Now, you want to talk bad COBOL - a common variant on the second loop is 
below.

100-PROCESS-EQUIP.
     FETCH FIRST EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-EQPP SET.
110-CONTINUE.
     IF ERROR-NUM NOT = ZEROES
         GO TO 199-X.
     PERFORM PROCESS-THIS-EQUIP.
     FETCH NEXT EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-EQPP SET.
     GO TO 110-CONTINUE.
199-X.
     EXIT.

There's an unnecessary paragraph (110-CONTINUE), they loop to the top 
before checking the error condition on the fetch, and they break the 
loop for *any* error condition, without checking for unexpected errors. 
  To me, that's bad COBOL.
```

```
In article <jKSdndgp06PBeTLbnZ2dnUVZ_qWtnZ2d@comcast.com>,
LX-i  <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

[snip]

>Who is the arbiter of "good" COBOL, or whether a coding style is a 
>"problem" or not?

The person who signs the checks for the project, of course.  In my 
experience this person is usually satisfied if:

1) All paragraphs are performed THRU a matching -EXIT with no intervening 
paragraph names between PARA-NAME and PARA-NAME-EXIT.

2) GO TO is allowed to refer only to the -EXIT paragraph immediately 
following, the PARA-NAME immediately preceding or the -ABEND routine for 
the program... in some instances LEAVE-THIS-PROGRAM (in subroutines) may 
be substituted for -ABEND.

>If someone's code does what it is supposed to do, and 
>they document it well, I really don't see the problem.

If the person who signs the check for a project is not smiling then there 
is a problem.

[snip]

>100-PROCESS-EQPP.
>     FETCH NEXT EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-ACFT SET.
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>Now, I don't particularly care for this style - but is it bad COBOL?

If ERROR-HANDLER is the -ABEND or the LEAVE-THIS-PROGRAM paragraph then it 
violates neither 1) nor 2) given above, therefore it would, in my 
experience, make most signers-of-checks smile.

[snip]

>It could be written 
>differently...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>I don't like it.

There is no -EXIT paragraph shown; this violates 1) above.  If 
ERROR-HANDLER is not (see above) then this violates 2).  Other folks might 
not smile, as well.

[snip]

>100-PROCESS-EQUIP.
>     FETCH FIRST EQPP-158 WITHIN UNIT-TO-EQPP SET.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>     EXIT.

Neither paragraph can be performed through its matching -EXIT, violating 
1).  GO TO range is outside of an immediately preceding paragraph or the 
matching -EXIT.  Someone who signs checks will, most likely, not be 
smiling.

DD
```

```
On Tue, 31 Jul 2007 19:39:34 -0600, LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote:

>Who is the arbiter of "good" COBOL, or whether a coding style is a 
>"problem" or not?  If someone's code does what it is supposed to do, and 
>they document it well, I really don't see the problem.

CoBOL is supposed to be self-documenting, and I do see that
maintenance programmers trust the code much more than the
documentation in my experience.

The useful part of documentation is saying "why", not "what".

If there is no maintenance, then documentation isn't needed, and
neither are programming styles designed to make mistake-free
maintenance easier.

But the CoBOL community isn't about one-time code.   We expect our
code to be maintained.   Our coding style should be designed to
optimize this process.
```

```
On Thu, 2 Aug 2007 15:17:35 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>It does raise some questions, though...
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>are not favourable to a future for COBOL.

Over time, the size of our components increases.   The electronics
technician replaces boards, not tubes.    If we repair our TV at all,
it's at this level - but more likely we throw it out.

Sure there are some people designing the transistors and assembly code
- but mostly we use components that we trust.   And some people still
cut their own two-by-fours to build a house, while others buy their
walls separately.

With CoBOL, our trustworthy components are pretty much at a line
number - old-timers who checked their assembly code are endangered.

If we create with larger components, we maintain with larger
components (or have disposable applications).     I expect this trend
to continue in the future.
```

```
On Aug 1, 5:47 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

> SECTION.
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> of assumptions! <G>

But the point is: THE COMPILER TOLD HIM.

The whole point of _actual_ encapsulation is that the compiler can
tell you when the programmer does something wrong, as in your eaxmaple
case.

In the case of sections with non-unique paragraph names the paragraph
had been put in the wrong section it may be that the name exists in
the right pargraph and the compiler silently uses that. The result is
that after spending a week of fruitless testing where the new code
just didn't work a day is spent combing through the code to find the
problem.*

I started by saying I was lazy. I want the compiler to tell me what I
did wrong before I waste my time testing something that won't work.


* Some years ago this exact thing happened to me. SECTION headings
were still in the program, but not PERFORMed, because it had
originally been segmented (overlayed) for MS-DOS (or possibly for CP/M
or MP/M before that). A new paragraph was added that accidentally
duplicated an existing name. The PERFORM did the wrong thing
_silently_.
```

```
On Aug 1, 5:47 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

> Following is a outline of the procedural pattern for implementing
> OOD in COBOL 85. I use braces to enclose the variable parts.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> a concept; not a how-to book.]

I think that you have managed to combine OO and procedural programming
in a way that eliminates all the advantages of both while retaining
all the disadvantages of each.

The whole point of 'reusable code' is that it can be reused without
copy-and-paste. You are building all the 'class' code into one
monolith by scattering their parts across all the sections while
simultaneously collecting all their data into one global block that is
Working-Storage.

If you wanted to reuse a 'class' in another program would you go
through picking out all the parts individually and adding the WHEN
clauses ?

The whole point of OOD is that it is 'O'rientated to the 'O'bject. All
the parts that relate to a class are collected together while you have
scattered them.

Implementing a subset of OOD in Cobol-85 is already catered for with
sub-programming. Granted it is necessary to pass a 'method' parameter
into the separate program that is called for each 'class'.

> My primary job responsibility, for more than 20
> years, was embedded systems programming.

That probably explains why you build single huge monolith programs
that accesses a global data area. The overhead of using CALL of
separate code pieces that were _actually_ encapsulated would be
abhorent.
```

- **References:** `<1185223084.744466.291540@22g2000hsm.googlegroups.com> <1185565019.612580.222200@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <eWrqi.35$IE5.12@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net> <qKvqi.105614$wG2.60480@newsfe17.lga> <1185584321.018963.77560@e9g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <13alet62ejv7f91@corp.supernews.com> <eg2sa3dfhomcm0nv9jd37o4h7ghq6eet45@4ax.com> <13asanp7chflce6@corp.supernews.com>`

```
On Jul 31, 6:14 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:

> Mr Brazee, if your compiler does not have EXIT SECTION,
> it may be simulated on any conforming COBOL 85 compiler.

By a GO TO ?

> Given that a "mini-program" may have a well-defined interface
> and its functionality may be implemented either as a section or
> as a paragraph group,

Neither are actually a 'mini-program'. For example they have no data
areas, those are still in the W-S and accessible to all other blocks
of code should they choose to do so. They are merely blocks of code.
It may well be that the program structure is such that they are
clearly isolated and the way of using them is defined.

> I submit that the difference may be
> characterized graphically as this: When implemented as a section,
> the "mini-program" is presented as a box made of a solid line,
> and, when implemented as a paragraph group, a box made of a
> dashed line.

Excuse me, but what exactly is drawing these 'lines', do you have a
special editing and/or printing function ? Or is is just your
imagination ? The compiler makes no such distinction, there is no
difference at all between a 'solid line' and a 'dotted line' in the
language.

> I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
> will treat each section as an entity.

No it won't.

> I chose "dashed line" to
> represent paragraph groups because the compiler will ignore
> the comments that may delineate the groups.

> Without the well-defined interface, one might as well surround
> everything with dotted lines.

You are confusing two quite distinct things. Yes, well defined
interfaces are a good thing. Yes, breaking down procedures into
smaller parts (paragraphs) is a good thing. But sticking on a SECTION
word while doing this is irrelevant, it adds nothing (except the
opportunity to do a GO TO). It does not do any actual 'encapsulation'
beyond catering for duplicate labels (which is not necessarily a good
thing).

> The choices are these: solid, dashed, or dotted!

If you want actual encapsulation and interfaces that are enforced
then:

Solid line = objects
Dashed line = separate programs
dotted line = sub-programs

sections don't even get a rating.
```

```
On Jul 31, 2:37 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

> > > I submit that the difference may be
> > > characterized graphically as this: When implemented as a section,
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> graphically as".

I am characterizing it as blank line.

> > > I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
> > > will treat each section as an entity.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> meaningless.

No. SECTIONs may be the basis of segmentation. Or they may be the
basis of a hierarchical program structure with a sub-structure of
paragraphs. Neither requires they be treated as an 'entity' any more
so than a paragraph is or a statement is. _You_ may be treating them
as an 'entity' with special privileges and attributes but the compiler
does not.

They _would_ be useful if there were some way to get the compiler to
complain if something outside the section performed a paragraph or
ensured that within a section only local paragraphs could be performed
or local data could be defined. In fact that is what sub-programs can
do, they are actually encapsulated, actually an entity.

> > You are confusing two quite distinct things. Yes, well defined
> > interfaces are a good thing. Yes, breaking down procedures into
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>     A. GO TO A.

OK, I'll correct myself: it adds the opportunity to do a GO TO to a
different paragraph within the section.

Whereas, for example:

        PERFORM ABC

    ABC-Header SECTION.    < or simply a comment
    ABC.
       PERFORM somepartofABC
       PERFORM anotherpartofABC
       .

    somepartofABC.
       ...
       ...
    anotherpartofABC.
       ...

does not add such an opportunity but is just as "mini-programish",
just as much an 'entity'
```

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1185865451.293160.20430@j4g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
> On Jul 31, 2:37 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> > "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
[snip]
> > > > I chose "solid line" to represent sections because the compiler
> > > > will treat each section as an entity.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> does not.

You seem to expressing your view of what an entity is
(or is not) and not what it is (or is not) to the compiler,
then you mistakenly attribute your view to that of the
compiler. This appears in "Neither requires they be
treated as an 'entity' ...", while the rules for COBOL
require that section headers be recognized and the
section and its contents be treated according to those
rules. [entity: being or existence, esp. when considered
as distinct or self-contained] A COBOL compiler will
recognize the existence of each section as something
distinct from all other sections.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Code problems with Perform Thru Exit causes fall through

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** spambait@milmac.com (Doug Miller)
- **Date:** 2007-07-31T23:52:00+00:00

```


"Doug Miller" <spambait@milmac.com> wrote in message 
news:_uRri.480$IE5.221@newssvr17.news.prodigy.net...
> In article <13avli6dnoucoa3@corp.supernews.com>, "Rick Smith" 
> <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Nope. Rick's right. It IS DISTINCT (and whether or not it is self-contained 
depends on the way the person writing the program has coded it).

You are also right that any paragraph within the SECTION can be PERFORMED 
from anywhere, but that is part of what is leading to the debate here.

If you consider a section to be a paragraph, then your comments and position 
are perfectly correct.

The point is, that many of us who use SECTIONs, DON'T see them as we would a 
paragraph. (this point was noted by Rick some days ago).

That means we DON'T perform paragraphs within them, from outside of them 
(or, in my case, even inside of them. I ONLY PERFORM a SECTION (or an inline 
perform, of course), or expect control to 'drop through', or any of the 
myriad other poor practices that apply to control in COBOL  programming.

You need to change your current perspective if you really want to understand 
why people use SECTIONed code. (But I suspect you don't ... your mind was 
made up long ago :-) So are the minds of most of the participants here (self 
included...); you don't seriously think for one minute that a bitter debate 
in CLC is going to change the way people code...?)

All of the pitfalls you are aware of are absolutely true if one considers a 
SECTION to be a paragraph (amongst other paragraphs)..

If one doesn't, then there are actually some benefits available from 
SECTIONed code.

One that hasn't been mentioned is that it is compliant with the COBOL 
hierarchy (Division>Section>Paragraph>Sentence>Statement) so it makes 
consistent logical sense to group code for local functions into Sections. I 
agree, that's a trivial one.

If you write it without paragraphs in it  (apart from the mandatory one at 
the start), then most of the objections you have evaporate.
You CAN'T use GO TOs or PERFORMs from inside or outside of it, so that looks 
like a step in the right direction. But, hang on a minute, then what you 
have a is just a big paragraph so why use a SECTION?

Well, why not? :-) It makes no difference, so why fight about it? It is 
simply a matter of what you prefer. Style. Pure and simple.

I use SECTIONs when coding COBOL much the same way as I use an outline view 
when writing an article or paper. It gives me a framework to hang the big 
ideas on and get them organised before attending to the nitty-gritty. I 
usually code the section headers as I identify the function which that 
particular section is going to be responsible for, then I put EXIT stubs in 
them so I can test the flow of control as it develops. I'd be the first to 
admit that this may be rooted in habits developed before such exotic 
constructs as EXIT SECTION  or EXIT PARAGRAPH were even thought of. 
Nevertheless, this approach works extremely well for me (and has done for 
nearly 40 years) but I am under no illusions about it being the ONLY way, or 
even the best way. (and I wouldn't consider shooting people who have other 
ideas :-))

As mentioned elsewhere, I'll fix whatever's broken, no matter how it's 
coded. And you won't hear me whingeing about it :-) (I might charge you more 
if what you give me is truly apalling :-))

Like most people, I code what I am COMFORTABLE with coding. (And compliant 
with shop standards, if it is code for someone else, that I have no control 
over.)

For you it's paragraphs, for me it's sections. You say "tomayto", I say 
"tomarto", it still doesn't change the flavour of the Bolognese... :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Aug 1, 3:41 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> > Fine -- it is neither distinct nor self-contained. All data is global, and
> > any
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> depends on the way the person writing the program has coded it).

But it is no more 'distinct' or 'self-contained' than similarly
constructed code _without_ the word SECTION (except as I pointed out
before the section allows for GOTO ~exit).

> You are also right that any paragraph within the SECTION can be PERFORMED
> from anywhere, but that is part of what is leading to the debate here.

Exactly, the claims that it is 'encapsulated' in some way by having
the word SECTION is misleading.

> If you consider a section to be a paragraph, then your comments and position
> are perfectly correct.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> SECTION to be a paragraph (amongst other paragraphs)..

You are using the words 'section' and 'paragraph' in ways that lead to
confusion. I don't know what you mean by "considers a SECTION to be a
paragraph". SECTIONs have the word SECTION, paragraphs don't.

> If one doesn't, then there are actually some benefits available from
> SECTIONed code.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the start), then most of the objections you have evaporate.

You should note that Rick specifically uses performed paragraphs
within the performed section in the mistaken expectation that this
makes it 'encapsulated'.

> You CAN'T use GO TOs or PERFORMs from inside or outside of it, so that looks
> like a step in the right direction. But, hang on a minute, then what you
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> simply a matter of what you prefer. Style. Pure and simple.

Actually there is an real difference. Certainly if all labels have the
word SECTION and there are no paragraph labels (there should be no
need for a dummy paragraph at the start and no exit paragraphs) then
there is no logical difference between that and the same program with
all the word SECTION removed.

The issue then is how can we be assured that this is the case. It
would be an error if one label was wrong by either missing its word
SECTION or having one spuriously, an error that may not cause enough
of a problem to be noticed in testing.

In the case of 'paragraph only' it is a simple grep or text search to
guarantee that the convention has been correctly followed. In the case
of 'section only' each label must be examined. I am lazy and choose
the easy way.

> I use SECTIONs when coding COBOL much the same way as I use an outline view
> when writing an article or paper. It gives me a framework to hang the big
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> them so I can test the flow of control as it develops.

Once you get over the 'I need an exit' habit (which _is_ a paragraph
within the section) then it can be seen that breaking down into
smaller blocks can be an advantage and the SECTION word is just
clutter.
```

```
> being distinct from all other imperitive statements.

So, while you initially denied that "the compiler will treat
each section as an entity", you now seem to be saying that
it will.
```

```
On Aug 1, 5:25 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

> > > > No. SECTIONs may be the basis of segmentation. Or they may be the
> > > > basis of a hierarchical program structure with a sub-structure of
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> Not at all.

Are you denying that you clipped it off or that it was deliberate ?

> The two elements that had to be shown were
> "requires" and "entity". I included the ellipsis to show that
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> necessary!

No. Wrong. You clipped it so that you didn't have to argue against
'any more so than ..', or 'with special privileges and attributes'.

First of all there is actually no demonstrated 'requirement' that the
compiler treat a section as an 'entity' at all. It certainly will
treat a group of sections with the same priority as being an 'entity'
when it is segmenting - it will generate different object blocks. It
may well change name spaces when it encounters a section header, but
otherwise it may be outputting just code.

Secondly the issue was that you were attempting to give some special
attributes and features to the SECTION word which you think recreated
the source as a 'mini=program', 'encapsulated' and 'solid line' when
this had nothing to do with it having the 'section' word and
everything to do with how you designed and wrote the program and could
be much the same whether it used the section word or not.

> > > [entity: being or existence, esp. when considered
> > > as distinct or self-contained] A COBOL compiler will
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> it will.

I denied that it would treat a section as an 'entity' that would earn
it a 'solid line'. In fact I will challenge you to examine the output
executable code from a compiler and identify where each section starts
and ends. If the compiler is treating it as 'distinct' then this
should be obvious.

I added the 'it will recognise each paragraph as something
distinct ...' in the hope that it would show you that your claim over
'entity' was trivial.

Whether a section is 'self-contained' in some way is not inherent in
it having a section keyword but is a feature of code designed to be
that whether it be a section or not.

But it certainly does not match my idea of 'self-contained' because
the data areas are not within it, as they can be with, say, sub-
programs.
```

```
> Denial of the former makes the latter irrelevant.

> the former because, as I explained, the omitted words were
> replaced by an ellipsis and, therefore, not "clipped off".

phrases. If the words have been ommitted they are 'clipped off'.

> I truly do not understand why you are raising this issue.
> The omitted words, in the original constext, suggest that
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> "any more so than", it seems to me, is still somewhat vague.

You were using 'entity' to attempt to support your claim about
sections giving some properties of 'encapsulation' as if it made it
like a sub-program. But you never even attempeted to explain why any
'treating it like an entity', if it does, did anything useful that
matched your claims.

> > No. Wrong. You clipped it so that you didn't have to argue against
> > 'any more so than ..', or 'with special privileges and attributes'.
>
> First, I did not clip it.

Well I looked at the quote you argued against and the words weren't
there. Your argument ignored those words.

> Second, there was no point in my arguing against "any more so
> than ..." since it seems to agree with my assertion.

You were supporting claims about sections by asserting that the
compiler treated them as an 'entity' as if this was something
different. If the compiler treats paragraphs, statements, words,
literals, full stops, divisions, and blank spaces as 'entities' then
your is a meaningless claim (as they all seem to be). It is a
distinction with no difference.

> Third, I have no idea what you mean by "with special privileges
> and attributes", which, it seems to me, may be an exaggeration
> of my actual claims.

Your claims were that the _compiler_ makes SECTIONs (with the keyword)
have the attributes of 'encapsulation', 'self-contained' and
'distinct'. None of which you have supported except by vague claims
that it is an 'entity' without explaining how this translates into
your claims.

Now I grant that your code may be contructed such that various parts
of the code do have those attributes, at least in concept, but this is
because of the way the code is implemented, not because it has a
keyword attached to it.

That is there is nothing in the compiler that enforces any of those
attributes. I can write SECTIONs that are _not_ 'encapsulated',
'distinct' or 'self-contained', therefore the compiler treating them
as an 'entity' (if it does) does not mean anything.

> Not "special attributes and features", just useful ones
> that are available only when using sections.

There are two things that a section (ignoring segmentation which is
the actual purpose) gives:

1) some resolving of duplicate paragraph names.
2) ability to use non-recursive GO TO within a perform scope (or
equivalent with EXIT ~).

Neither are what I would call useful, neither are unique to sections.
Neither support your claim of 'encapsulation' or 'self-contained',
neither enforce an interface. Neither justify a 'solid line'.

In fact you said that you didn't need to use GO TO: """without exit
paragraphs or GO TO statements."""

> Well that's a problem! You are treating "solid line" in absolute
> terms. I am treating "solid line" versus "dashed line" in relative
> terms as the comparison of sections versus paragraph groups.

No. It is _you_ that is treating 'solid line' as something other than
your imaginings. You were attempting to illustrate 'encapsulation' and
'self-contained' as if the SECTION keyword made this happen.

> Then you seem close to realizing that the compiler will recognize
> each section and each paragraph as things distinct;

And each word, each full-stop, and each statement.

> but will not
> recognize any paragraph group, at all. Given that, which of the
> two: section or paragraph group, is more deserving of the "solid
> line" and which of the "dashed line"?

Whether a particular piece of code deserves a 'solid line' a 'dotted
line' or 'skull and crossbones' is a result of how well it is designed
and coded and not whether it has the SECTION keyword. Neither sections
nor groups of paragraphs enforce nor assist with any of what you have
claimed.

_Neither_ deserve any more than a box made of question marks because
the compiler will not tell you where the problems are.

With sub-programs you can get some _actual_ encapsulation, they are an
'entity' where it makes a difference. You get some _actual_ self-
containment, even to the point of nesting. They deserve a dotted line
because they still can access GLOBAL item.

Apart from the trivial and error-prone non-detection of duplicate
names the only actual difference between a similarly coded SECTION and
group of paragraphs is that the former allows GO TO _different_
paragraph name within the scope of the perform.

Which is what I originally said.

Just to remind you about your erroneous claims, the "special
attributes" that you claimed for the SECTION keyword were:

"""Well, no, that is not the 'only' purpose! Another is
encapsulating mini-programs as parts of a larger program.
This is similar to nested programs; but without the call
overhead or the structuring requirements for contained
and common programs."""

You may get [some of] the _appearance_ of nested programs in the
source by arranging your code in a particular way but you get none of
the benefits of them regardless of whether it has a SECTION keyword or
not.
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1186085255.177114.263120@e16g2000pri.googlegroups.com...
> On Aug 3, 4:22 am, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> So, you agree that 'encapsulating' doesn't actually happen.

I agree that a purpose of sections does not include
encapsulating [the source codes of] mini-programs;
but still hold that a purpose of sections may include
encapsulating [the functionalities of] mini-programs.

> > And please note that *I* never used the term "special
> > attributes".
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> So, you agree they are not 'similar to nested programs'.

"... whether it has a SECTION keyword or not." and
"... they are not ...."?

In the context for the original was: This [purpose for
sections] is similar to [using] nested programs [for the
same purposes]; .... Where "purpose for sections"
was implied, "using" was omitted, and "for the same
purposes" was loosely implied by "mini-programs".
That is, I never intended "[Sections] is similar to
nested programs; ...."

[It might also have been construed as "This [purpose
for sections] is similar to [the purpose for] nested
programs; ...." That construction would be denied by
"mini-programs".]

I hold that sections encapsulating [the functionalities of]
mini-programs as parts of a larger program are similar to
[using] nested programs [for the same purposes].
However, using nested programs together has certain
problems, which were mentioned, previously.

I accept that there are cases where required functionalities
can not be implemented in mini-programs and, in those
cases, there can be no similarity to nested programs.
```

```
On Aug 3, 3:40 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
>
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
> [using] nested programs [for the same purposes].

Yet above you agreed that none of the befefits accrue: "That I get
none of the benefits is acceptable".

Sticking the keyword SECTION on a bunch of paragraphs is similar to
not sticking on the keyword except the keyword allows you to use GO TO
from one paragraph to another (which you don't) plus adds the bug-trap
of not reporting when the same name is used twice.

Anything else is pure puffery.

> However, using nested programs together has certain
> problems, which were mentioned, previously.

Which were, IIRC, the 'overhead' of keying in a few headings plus
alleged reduced performance.  Have you actually done a test between:

     MOVE stuff to interface
     PERFORM something
     MOVE interface to stuuf

and:

     CALL something USING stuff

where something is a nested program ?

When I tested this with an optimising compiler I found the difference
to be insignificant.

Not that I use nested programs, preferring the flexability and
reusability of separate programs and not caring about a few
microseconds.


> I accept that there are cases where required functionalities
> can not be implemented in mini-programs and, in those
> cases, there can be no similarity to nested programs.
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:1186116918.928634.279660@x40g2000prg.googlegroups.com...
> On Aug 3, 3:40 pm, "Rick Smith" <ricksm...@mfi.net> wrote:
> > "Richard" <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > > > source by arranging your code in a particular way but you get none
of
> > > > > the benefits of them regardless of whether it has a SECTION
keyword or
> > > > > not.
> >
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> none of the benefits is acceptable".

I see that as a logical conclusion:
    no nested programs = no benefits of
    no nested programs = no problems of

> Sticking the keyword SECTION on a bunch of paragraphs is similar to
> not sticking on the keyword except the keyword allows you to use GO TO
> from one paragraph to another (which you don't) plus adds the bug-trap
> of not reporting when the same name is used twice.

I do not design with GO TO in mind; but will use GO TO,
within the same section, when I suspect it will significantly
improve performance.

> Anything else is pure puffery.
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> where something is a nested program ?

I have not done timed peformance, yet. About nine years
ago, I did have a program that was entirely nested; that is,
each nested program had one unnamed paragraph. I did
that to take advantage of local variables. Performance was
noticibly slower so I ripped it all out. The program currently
uses single paragraph sections, with two exceptions, and the
few parameters that were passed before are now moved
before the perform. One of the exceptions is a five
paragraph mini-program I added several months ago. I am
satisfied with the overall performance of the program and
probably will not modify it for quite some time.

I suspect that I may, over the next few days, modifiy a
different program (a smaller one I don't really care about)
to get some timing data. That program was built with
multi-paragraph sections; but without the "well-defined
interfaces" of mini-programs.

> When I tested this with an optimising compiler I found the difference
> to be insignificant.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> > cases, there can be no similarity to nested programs.
```

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

> > > > > > > You may get [some of] the _appearance_ of nested programs in
the
> > > > > > > source by arranging your code in a particular way but you get
none
> > of
> > > > > > > the benefits of them regardless of whether it has a SECTION
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
> similar is ... well, just puffery really.

[H'm, "just" versus "unjust" puffery? <g>]

Until the other day, I did not know what "puffery" was.
My dictionary defines it as: undue or exaggerated praise,
as in publishing.

"[S]imilar to" is a comparative. In this case, I intended it
to help the reader imagine what a mini-program might do;
so "similar to" is "not the same as" or, simply "not".
I could also have said "similar to using C source files";
but there are more differences to explain (how extern and
static data and functions relate to COBOL) and that would
be less helpful than using a COBOL referent.

Because I see and experiment with different ways for
using COBOL, the comments, often made about
performing single paragraphs only, seem, to me, to be
pufferies.

>
> > > Sticking the keyword SECTION on a bunch of paragraphs is similar to
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> maintainability, and development time, in persuit of them.

Needing without knowing how seems fruitless.
Being prepared to sacrifice, does not mean sacrifice.


> > > Anything else is pure puffery.
```

```

"Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote in message
news:13b5mvvh10i8se0@corp.supernews.com...
>
> "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:1186116918.928634.279660@x40g2000prg.googlegroups.com...
[snip]
> > Which were, IIRC, the 'overhead' of keying in a few headings plus
> > alleged reduced performance.  Have you actually done a test between:
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> > to be insignificant.

Using Micro Focus COBOL 3.2.24, I ran much
simpler tests than I had previously planned; but the
results are more reflective of 'overhead'.

Data that was passed on the 'call' to the nested
program was moved to, then from, the mini-program
interface, except in the last test where only a result
was obtained. In that case, only the move from the
interface was done; but each 'program' also did an
internal move. The ratios given are nested program
to mini-program (NP:MP).

8-bytes (to and from) 11.5:1
80-bytes (to and from) 3:1
80-bytes (global), 2-byte (result) 200:1

I would not call any of these insignificant.
```

```
On Aug 4, 3:02 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> <snipped Tweedledum and Tweedledee exchange reminiscent of  two elephants
> fighting over a currant bun (the very small kind that has a scarcity of
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> lead punch girl:-)

Been there, done that, still have one here (though obviously not the
same one as yours).

> This was long before segmentation was available and the compiler ran under
> TOS (Tape Operating System, disk drives still being a phenomenon confined to
> laboratories.)

Actually my references have it that segmentation was in COBOL-61 and
it may have been earlier versions, but also may have been optional.

XE13 was a '61 compiler and certainly had segmentation on tape loaded
programs. ICL TP4118 "Introduction to COBOL", Jan 1969, is based on
Cobol'61 and JOD 65 has segementation


I recall one program that was badly coded  (not by me) with respect of
performing a SECTION and reloaded the overlay for each tape record.
The programmer had done something like:

     PERFORM Read-Tape UNTIL EOF

Where it was READ-TAPE SECTION 65.

"""This concept, originally implemented for assembly languages, was
very early incorporated in COBOL; the language features have changed
only in name in ANS 68 and ANS 74."""

"""Only two, very simple language features are invovled in
segmentation: the use of SECTION headers to partition the Procedure
Division, and the optional SEGMENT-LIMIT clause in the OBJECT-COMPUTER
paragraph."""
```

```


<snip>
> In 1966, I was using a 360/30 with three 2311 disk drives with
> removable packs with about 7.2 megabytes per pack.  We had DOS.
>>
Yes, Clark, we moved up to DOS  not long after this. We moved off COBOL 59 
at the same time. It would have been around 67/68 (I have slightly addled 
memories of the 60s... can't think why... :-))

It was in New Zealand. The cost of these devices was prohibitive for us.

It was also our perception that these devices were still "experimental" and 
we really didn't understand how to use them. I remember one of my colleagues 
coding a COBOL "CLOSE WITH REWIND" for a disk file when we finally got to 
using them. :-)

I also remember an ICL executive at another place I worked, remarking when 
the new disk drives were demonstrated to him: "Bloody dustbin lids... 
they'll never sell..."

Ah, hindsight...:-)

Pete
```

```
It doesn't prevent the idiot from screwing up and inserting code in

idiot from doing so by compartmentalising code and explicitly
PERFORMing each SECTION.

time ago) and personally prefer SECTIONs. If you are comfy with
PERFORM...THRU then I am happy with that.
```

```
On Jul 28, 7:36 am, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> On Jul 24, 8:38 am, Lizette Koehler <stars...@mindspring.com> wrote:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > with a missint END-IF/

Further to that another area is if each label is supposed to be a
SECTION header.

If you have code that is
   MAIN SECTION.

    PERFORM A THRU A-exit
    ..

   A SECTION.
     ...
        GO TO A-exit
     ...
   A-exit SECTION.
     EXIT.
   B.

Then the A-exit section includes the paragraph B so this is part of
the perform scope.
```
